RATING: 5
The geometric permutations in turn choices and future planning that occur with cards lending strength is a *fantastic* feature and I would argue the central masterpiece of the game. Since you'll only see a fraction of the cards in any given game, game engines are rarely even close to being the same which is really nice. Great game at 1 and 2 players, 3 is good as well (4 drags, and 5 is a death sentence in terms of game speed, even the rule book sort of warns against it). The mental oompf required to handle the limited/governed engine building and the resource management lends itself to players occasionally developing a dire case of the "so smarts" (where you "were thinking you were so smart that you were going to do XYZ only to miscalculate what you actually needed"). 

The two greatest dings I have against it stem from time and cards. This is a game of "who can get the best combo engine going fastest and then ride that super-hard." That the game takes way longer than you expect it to, and I've seen 3p games run as much as 2.5 hours at the top end... Second, if everyone doesn't stay together, the last third/quarter of the game is a waste (and it should be called) which is rather anti-climactic, especially if someone goofs or it's taking a long time to run through. In some ways, I'd almost just concede if there was some form of honor in that, and I think this is why you don't seen engine-building games that let you run the engine for a long time because you just build the gap in terms of positioning. Sort of an oddity, it's possible (but I would argue not probable) to ignore the extra characters in terms of acquiring them and still get to victory. I keep tinkering with that, but find the longitudinal planning of what you want your engine to be early enough in the game to take advantage of it exceedingly difficult (especially with card wiping and new cards coming out). A minor ding might be that it's only interactive in terms of the resource draft (which is most needed and manageable at 2p, above that and it's sort of negligible and turns into a multi-player solitaire game of sorts). In general, a 4 or 5, occasionally hitting 6, but not as a rule of thumb.

RATING: 6
[I]tl;dr A race game meets a Euro worker-placement meets a hand management. A unique blend of genres, which makes Lewis and Clark stand out from the crowd.[/I]. 
[COLOR=#CCCCFF]____________________________[/COLOR]

[b]Read the full review [url=https://boardgamegeek.com/thread/1608929/exploring-louisiana-purchase-cutthroat-style]here[/url].[/b]

Lewis and Clark is an interesting Euro game because it's not like anything else out there. The race mechanic along the edge of the board is punishing and engaging, and there are many different ways to skin the cat. 

Lewis and Clark is built on three main pieces: hand management, worker placement on the game board, and dragging your exploratory camp farther and farther upriver, until you finally reach the long-hoped-for Pacific Ocean. 

Generally I don't like AP-prone, solitaire type games, but Lewis and Clark is an exception to that rule, and I also ended up enjoying it solo. I was surprised at what a slick system it is. Alexander Mckenzie makes for a great opponent -- he moves along so steadily that it really adds tension to every single one of your moves. I already enjoy the puzzle-like aspect of Lewis and Clark, so that was a fantastic addition to a game I already enjoyed. 

Playing solo, there's less pressure for character cards and spaces on the board, which I kind of enjoyed but also kind of wished for the interaction of the regular game. I can already see at player counts the huge importance of the on-board action slots, as they don't cost you time at encampment -- but may cost you Indians! I can see how this game would be brutal at four or five players. 

Overall, Lewis and Clark is an interesting game, but I think a bit too brain-burner for my tastes. Everything takes so much thought that you will take forever to play your cards. And with three or four players, that can make for a long, long game. Don't get me wrong, Lewis and Clark is fun -- but I'm just not sure I'd still pick it over other, better midweight games in my collection like [thing=96913][/thing] or [thing=157354][/thing].

RATING: 6.5
One of the reasons why Lewis and Clark is on this list is because it brings friends to the table that would otherwise not be too interested in board games. The theme goes a tremendous way towards elevating the mechanics and even introducing some elements that twist the genre of worker placement a little bit.

While the race aspect can have a win/lose feel to it, there are some definite photo finishes that take place which makes the game feel very tight. The hand-building is a fun way of fine-tuning your crew as you race to avoid the trappings of lost time on your way down the trail. It can be very difficult to decide which actions to take, though, and whether or not building the engine that can take you to the end will actually slow you down in the long run. It's a game that is much heavier than it would seem at first glance, if only because the decisions are tough and there is a bit of a struggle to know what the best decision may be at any given time. 

Lewis and Clark is a very well-crafted game with a unique puzzly feeling. At times it can feel like the game is too overstuffed with ideas to make upgrading your crew viable, especially when you're tasked with racing your opponents. After several plays, though, you'll start to understand the feeling of the game and take risks that may end up paying off later. I really enjoy Lewis and Clark for the different experience it provides, despite the slightly unwieldy feeling it offers.

RATING: 6
2 plays / 4, 5 players

LIKE: Lots of forward thinking decisions, with an engine-building mix of cards and worker placements. The "Indians" use is interesting how that resource is shared and used for worker placement, and it creates an interesting timing dynamic. Great artwork.

DISLIKE: My major complaint is downtime between turns. The game can be brain-burning on a first play, which slows some players. Planning card purchases was chaotic since cards would move off the board quickly. Playing with 5 people took 4 hours with tremendous downtime, so I don't recommend it.

OVERALL: A good game, but I wasn't blown away by it because I had played Rococo and Concordia before this, which both use a similar card mechanism, and I enjoy those more. The player interaction is ok, and for a few turns I didn't feel any interaction at all. Collecting the Indians in the pow-wow was very interesting if well-timed. I'm rating this average (6) for the downtime, but over time I could see this getting better when strategies start to emerge.

RATING: 6
*Rating based on one play.

Lewis & Clark is a solid game mechanically, but has a few elements that clash with my gaming preferences.  First, the game is very tense... mostly.  I'm not quite sure what causes it, maybe the feeling it gives of being a race, but this game felt very tense, even to the point of being stressful.  I think the tension also comes from loss aversion.  In this game, you are going to make progress with your scout, but you will almost certainly lose some of that progress when you camp.  If you have a strong feeling of loss aversion (you hate to lose progress you worked hard to make), this game might not be for you.

While the early and mid game is tense, unfortunately the end game is not.  In the last few rounds, once I had settled on my plan to finish, there was no tension as there was nothing I could do to push things further.  I knew when I would finish, and I just had to hope that no one could finish before then.  This led to my second concern about the game.

This game is just asking for analysis paralysis.  There is so much forethought and planning required for this game that it becomes easy to go into mental lockdown trying to figure out the most efficient plan.  And even once you have a plan in place, your opponents' actions can cause your plan to go completely out the window, leaving you to grind your mental gears over a new plan.  The thinking required in this game makes the game play much longer than it could with less forward planning required.  This may not be a concern to everyone, but it is a concern to some.

Finally, for me, the way the theme was applied to the game was a bit of a turn off.  In the game, you can gain Native Americans to your party and then spend them for actions in a Native American village.  This made me uneasy as it felt like I was taking advantage of the Native Americans, using them up and then disposing of them, much as Europeans and white Americans have done for centuries.  I was fine with the ability to recruit Native American cards into your hand because you can do the same with white frontiersmen and it feels like the Native Americans are equals, but using Native Americans as a resource felt dirty to me.

A quick note on components: the copy I played was apparently a second printing, which was cheaper than the first, and it showed.  The resources didn't have stickers and were instead plain wooden pieces, which is something I'm used to from many Euro games.  The card thickness was a bit less from what I understand, which is fine since the cards don't get shuffled much and can easily be sleeved.  The biggest component issue was the lack of any kind of player aid in this version.  This game really could use a player aid to spell out a player's options for their turn, which I believe the first printing had but was missing from the second.

In the end, I feel like L&C is probably a good game and I can see the appeal it holds for many people, but it's just not one that appeals to me.

RATING: 8

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.83
FULL DISCLOSURE: I worked as the editor on the English rules. If you're playing Lewis&Clark in English, and something isn't clear in the rules, you know whom to blame!

Is it a hand management game? Yes, if you want to focus on that! Is it a worker placement game? Yes, if you want to focus on that! One of the most important strategic decisions that you'll have to make is this very thing: where should I focus my efforts? Generally, building up spiffy card combos is the way to go; but they're costly. They also tend to benefit your immediate neighbours as well as yourself. And the card combos might be terrific for getting you up river, but there are mountains ahead, which need very different resources...and after that there's more river!  Placing your Indian workers is easier, but there is no guarantee they'll come back to you.

Early on, as you're trying to build up resources and figuring out what characters you want to nab, it can be just a tad on the dry side. However, by mid-game, it becomes exceedingly tense, and only grows more so as the game progresses. You might still be building up your resources when you come to the horrified realization that your opponents are madly dashing ahead of you. Since it is a race game, it will be apparent when the end is imminent, and then there will be a sprint to the finish between the leading players; and it tends to be close between first and second! Can you easily recover from mistakes? Yes, if the mistake was made early on; late-game mistakes, however, are utterly disastrous!

M. Dutrait's art is simply magnificent. Each of the eighty-four cards is utterly unique, as are their abilities. The only exception to this is the starting hands: each player's hand has exactly the same abilities as the opponents', but every character is a separate character from the historical expedition. Most of the time, their abilities actually are related to the historical personage. 

It's incredible: Cédrick Chaboussit set out to create a game about a rural village in France in 1900, and re-themed it to the Lewis&Clark expedition, but the theme does not feel at all pasted on! I have no idea how he managed to pull this one off. Immersively themed? No. But well-themed (for a Euro), most definitely.

It's highly unlikely that you'll get through anywhere near a full deck during a game, so it has tremendous replayability; and special markers are included to alter the "course" lay-out, if you want to try something a tad different. The solitaire game is tremendously painful: you struggle to organize things to make great advances, while your fictitious opponent does nothing each turn BUT advance! And the solitaire version is tense from about the second or third turn!

At this early stage in my plays, I think 2 or 3 players might be best, with three the very best; but we'll see how things develop. UPDATE: Yep, three is the best and two is almost as good; and the solitaire version is almost as good as two-player!

RATING: 4
Is the card play clever? Of course it's clever. Does the artistic theme and setting jump right out at you? Of course it does. Is the game original? Seems that way. What's not to love? It's not super fun. It's honestly a bit convoluted. And all the drama is building up to these massive almost game breaking swings of movement on the race track. It's okay. I don't love it. I honestly just wish it was a co-op. I would rather explore North America with friends than competitors. The game dynamic is eh.

RATING: 8.5
Clever engine-builder with a unique end condition that scales very well from two to four players.  It's a bit long, but most people have figured it out quickly so game length is mostly dependent on AP.  I've noticed a lot of different strategies showing up, and players have noticeably improved after each game.

The scaling of the symbols means the game is probably most interesting with 3, as a few cards are way weaker with 2 than with 3/4/5.  Not played with 5, but I think it would be too long.  However, game plays perfectly well with 2p otherwise and collection doesn't feel broken.

Only real issue: card quality is very flimsy.  Sleeved with FFG sleeves, somewhat slippery (I prefer ultra pro but they were too thick) but don't bend too much and feel a bit heftier.  Significant improvement.

RATING: 7
Lewis & Clark combines what I'm learning to call handbuilding (the central mechanic) with resource conversion and worker placement, so I should have loathed it.  However, what you do with the cards bears little resemblance to Dominion, all those mechanisms are really well integrated and it has reasonable fidelity to its theme.  For instance, every card represents an historical character and rather than collect prestige points, it's a sort of race along rivers and over mountains to Oregon. The one drawback is that playing time is quite long with a fair bit of down time.  I might try to limit future plays to no more than three players.

The artwork is lovely and production quality is good.

RATING: 9
Very fulfilling game but due to number of turn options available, best played with 3 players maximum.  Severe AP possibilities with wrong players.  Initial impression was that this was a race game.  But the race is up the Saint Louis (score track) river, but this is more than a score track with the tricky river to mountains switches and the steps back for setting up camp arising from surplus natives, goods and cards.  There are subtle hand management possibilities if you get rid of your one value cards and clear ones as to whether to use your cards for the action or as an Indian(s).  The flexibility of being able to make camp and/or recruit before or after our compulsorily action is pleasing.  The artwork has character but I thought could be a little clearer.  The counter sticker images are a tad small and the brown card symbols front and back different shades.  Using bison for water movement was not intuitive, but these are minor niggles on what was an unexpected gem.  Congratulations to Cédrick Chaboussit and Ludonaute on a very impressive publication.

RATING: 8
Great recipes are made with good ingredients, not new exotic ones. Lewis & Clarke is definitely a great game, even without showcasing new mechanics, but mixing known ones in a tight, exciting and, as a whole, innovative design.

The game is a race along a river (and few mountains) to reach the west coast. There's a bit of worker placement (with a twist: workers placed in the common board are "lost", and the first player using the retrieving action "steals" all of them), a bit of deck building (everybody starts with the same 6 cards, but the other 54 cards in the game are all unique!), a little resource management (and the fleet is quite small: accumulating resources can be quite painful), and definitely an aspect of tempo management.

As I already said, everything amalgamates in a tight, challenging game, that plays in a quite unique way.

Initial tentative rating is a 8.

RATING: 8
Initial Impression: Wow! This is an amazing game. I was initially attracted to it by the wonderfully colorful artwork. The board, the cards,... it all looks so nice. The promise of a "deckbuilder" also attracted me. I was initially thrown off by the fact that your hand contains your entire deck from which to choose what to play. Acquiring cards allows for more powerful actions and the decisions to use a card either for its action or to power other actions is wonderful. The race aspect of the game ensures that the players have little time to tune their hands/tableaus. Efficiency will win the game. I'm looking forward to getting in more plays of this game.

RATING: 8

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
L&C is a card-based engine-building euro-game that ties card drafting into its game play and that ties its length to a victory point goal (reach a point on the board) instead of a set number of rounds. A player must make both tactical and strategic decisions on each turn. There is potential for interaction between players and there is a decent amount of variability between plays. That combination would normally result in a high rating from me; however, the pace of Lewis & Clark feels way too slow for what you get out of it. 

In the end, L&C pales in comparison to other games due to the mechanisms mixing together in a way that makes it difficult to pre-plan your turn. Each player is constantly reevaluating the board and strategy, up until the point when the transition to engine firing occurs. It should be noted that if a player fails to build their engine efficiently or fails to account for the pace at which the other players are moving, a player may not even reach the engine-firing phase of the game. If I want a tactical hand management game with card drafting, I'll play Concordia over this. If I want a deep strategy game with variability and some race aspects, I'll play Great Western Trail over this. If I want a card-based strategic euro game with resource management, I'll play Agricola over this. If I want an interactive euro with card drafting, I'll play Food Chain Magnate over this.  

The historical nature of Lewis & Clark is great. However, the production hides some of the thematic flavor in the rule book. The down time between turns in Lewis & Clark would be more acceptable if the flavor text for each character was on the card instead of the rule book (this would give you something to do while you wait). Passing around the rule book is a poor substitute.

The appealing mechanisms, decent solo variant and ability to modify the mountain/river track, bumps this to a 6.5 from about a 6. I'd never play this game with more than 3 players.

RATING: 7.5
This game is a blend of a whole lot of mechanics but it all works seamlessly together. I love that the victory point track is re-fashion into a 'race', makes it much more thematic but also my gripe. The sweet spot for the game will probably be 3 players, anymore than that and it'll be too long of a game and lesser than that will make it less tense. Components and artwork are really great as well for the game!

The gripe I have with it is there's nothing you can do to stop the winner if they're about to win. Usually Euro games do just fine with this cause there's a lot of end game scoring so you don't exactly know who's the winner when game ends. For Lewis and Clark, you know exactly who's going to win so it's just a matter of waiting for the person to end the game. Maybe repeated plays with experienced players will create a more tense finish!

Present from Mervyn.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Quickly became a favorite after just a few plays. Having to use cards from your hand to power up other card abilities is genius. I understand it's not alone in having this mechanic, but it is done so well. Each decision is met with a penalty, whether losing a card as a power up, or losing an Indian to the village, that you must account and plan for, and does make for some AP proneness. However, unlike AP prone games like Agricola and Five Tribes, every option is interesting, and sometimes exciting. But maybe the game's greatest asset isn't in it's mechanics, but in it's winning condition - be the first to the Pacific. Unlike victory-point salad games where you can only hope you're doing well enough to win, being able to focus on a single most-important goal, and being able to see exactly how you're doing against your opponents is just incredible. I've heard L&C compares to racing games, which is great because I was looking for a good racing game! And this just might be the best out there. The artwork is fantastic, as are the components. If I had one complaint it would be that the game board itself could have been of higher quality. I don't see it remaining in tip-top shape past a few setups, which is a bummer because L&C is going to see a lot of plays in my house. Awesome, awesome game.

RATING: 6
Lewis and Clark employs a hodgepodge of mechanics. You have worker placement, resource management, variable player power, quasi-deck building and a race game. Despite this jumble, the design works. The components are adequate and the art gorgeous. 

I was enthused on my first few plays of the game, but in my postweaning plays the game settled to mediocrity. That's not to say that the game is not innovative or enjoyable, but it could drag and be repetitive at times. Perhaps it's scaleability has something to do with it. I would consider that this scales the best at three since four or five can drag a bit especially with newcomers or AP prone players. 

All in all, a great design with a great theme and engaging gameplay.

RATING: 7
An unusual deck-building and "race" game.  Efficient play and timing are critical for both deck-building and resource collection/storage.  I'm not fond of the fact that the cards available for purchase are all unique and become available randomly (instead of having a layered/stacked deck with more powerful cards reserved for later rounds), but I may grow to like the game despite these minor quibbles.  Note to self: get a multipurpose card or two early and trash some of the starter cards ASAP.  The starters seem useful/necessary, but they really slow down your game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Only just got to play this gem. It's clear why this game gets good ratings. It has all the makings of a classic. The mechanics are clean and simple, yet there are a ton of options from turn to turn. Low setup time, replayability is high and the art is amongst the best I've seen. A euro game where the theme actually comes through for a change.

Gameplay wise it requires a fair bit of brain churning. It's an exercise in optimisation and you have to plan well upfront. And while the planning seems euro-style solitaire it's every bit as important to gauge your opponents actions because failing to time your actions right can be the difference between getting ahead or falling behind. It can be quite unforgiving at times.

There is a bit of luck involved in when certain character cards become available, but overall it comes down to skill and knowing how to take advantage of the options provided. The 30 minutes per player stated on the box seem overly optimistic to me. Two hours for a three player game is more likely and if it's your first or second play I'd say even 50 minutes per player.

Edit: Bumped it to a 9. This one deserves it.

RATING: 9
I love engine building games and this one may be the best of the bunch.  It involves hand management, worker placement and deck building.  It strikes a nice balance between letting you build the engine to your desires but yet still not completely opening it up into a sandbox game.  The game has a 5 card buy row, a penalty for hoarding to many resources and not playing all of your cards.  These confine you to keep your engine lean but at the same time don't slow it down too much.  My favorite thing about this game is the scoring track.  It is not only a race instead of just a tracker but it also makes your engine have to be capable of 2 separate types of movement, which creates an interesting puzzle.

RATING: 9

Wasn't expecting to like this at all - as it was described to me as a blend of the 'best' euro mechanics in a euro game ... and my tastes tend to thematic.

But it's great! Yes it's a euro but the theme makes sense and helps the game - and there is bags of reply with the cards and the terrain tokens supplied so you can alter the river if you really want.

Not as solo as it first appears, as you interact based on the cards your neighbours have played (like 7 wonders) and spots taken in the village (worker placement) and the cards you draft.

All in all a favourite for now. Could suffer from downtime, but at the moment I need the time to consider options so it doesn't feel long, even though it is.

We'll see if this awakening continues for Lewis and Clark.

RATING: 7
A very interesting, surprisingly difficult game that crippled me with AP more than usual. It is quite a challenge to puzzle out how to use your cards, Native Americans, and resources as efficiently as possible, with little to no waste. Add to this the fact that you always need to be thinking a couple of [i]rounds[/i] (not turns!) in advance, to see how far you will travel, how many spaces you will move back when camping, and what kinds of cards you'll need in future rounds to get through new terrain. Add to this the fact that you're constantly monitoring what cards your opponents have in their tableaux, trying to predict when they are going to camp, and deciding whether you want lots of goods or very few based on the number of symbols on the table... Suddenly you have a huge number of things to consider! Some of my turns were so long that my husband started to get frustrated, so I hope with future plays I'll be able to speed things up.

RATING: 7
Lewis and Clark is a neat combination of worker placement and also some mild deck building that allows you to modify your capabilities throughout the game, that makes it feel pretty distinct from any other game I've played.

The scoring is also fairly unique, in that players are trying to advance up the river, setting up base camps to save their progress and reset their hands of cards, trying to be the first to set up the final base camp at the mouth of the river.  So, the scoring appears as a race between players, which is neat.  Everything you do in the game is with advancement in mind.

The game is very much a get stuff to turn into other stuff which allows you to do this other stuff sort of game.  It's very much about optimization; getting the right amount of this resource so I can do this other stuff.  Being off by just one can potentially set you back several turns against your competition, so you need to be rather precise about everything you do.

Overall, just a neat, rather fresh game that I look forward to playing more of!

RATING: 6
The gameplay is a little too fiddly for me. Once again, there are lots of cards with different abilities (displayed with dizzying iconography) so card combos are important. And this setup (with lots of cards that don't all come out and where you have limited lookahead) has the inherent problem of hoping cards pop up that mesh with your cards/plans.

That said, there are a lot of good things going on in this game. I see some comments about it being solitaire and I very much disagree with this. It is critical to understand when your opponents are going to camp and  pow-wow, and the meat of the game is timing things to maximize your leeching from opponents and minimize their leeching of you. The double use of the cards leads to some very interesting and tense decisions and how to play your hand. It's a very innovative and fresh feeling game. And in today's marketplace, that is saying something.

I'm thinking 3 player might be best. The game has a per/player playing time, probably 40 minutes per player. Plus, there is the 7 Wonders issue, where it plays better to be in contact with all other players for card effects. And the board can get locked up with more players before pow-wows and it might free things up a bit. Would like to try again with 3. 

Update: raised to 7. Played a 5 player game in well less than 2 hours, so that is very encouraging. Length concerns seem unwarranted. Very interesting decisions and the package does feel fresh. I liked it a lot more the 2nd time.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
[b]AVOID THE RULES[/b] ([COLOR=#FF0000][b]A V[/b][/COLOR]ideo [COLOR=#FF0000][b]O[/b][/COLOR]f [COLOR=#FF0000][b]I[/b][/COLOR]nstruction & [COLOR=#FF0000][b]D[/b][/COLOR]emonstration [COLOR=#0000FF][b]T[/b][/COLOR]hat [COLOR=#0000FF][b]H[/b][/COLOR]elps [COLOR=#0000FF][b]E[/b][/COLOR]ase [COLOR=#FF0000][b]R[/b][/COLOR]ule [COLOR=#FF0000][b]U[/b][/COLOR]nderstanding & [COLOR=#FF0000][b]L[/b][/COLOR]earning - [COLOR=#FF0000][b]E[/b][/COLOR]asy [COLOR=#FF0000][b]S[/b][/COLOR]tart!)

[u]BGG:[/u] http://boardgamegeek.com/video/42090/lewis-clark/lewis-clark-avoid-the-rules-by-connect-more 
[u]YouTube:[/u] https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=BUfZMwrJt7k

[thing=140620][/thing] is a game that rewards clever [b]hand management[/b], strategic [b]worker placement[/b], and efficient [b]hand/deck building[/b] (through [b]card drafting[/b]). A keen sense of timing is required to know the optimal moment when to perform and apply your actions. The seamless integration of these mechanics and the historic theme make this game a huge hit for me!

I get the same feeling as playing [thing=2651][/thing] (which is awesome!) but this one is richer and (for me) thematically superior. Instead of "racing" to be the person to power the most cities, you are racing upstream and over mountains to be the first to the Pacific Coast (Oregon I am guessing). In Power Grid you have to carefully plan each turn right from the very beginning to ensure that you have all the money required to execute your plans. In L&C all of your maneuvers are mainly controlled by your deck of cards. 

Everyone starts with the same deck, but this can be quickly modified by recruiting new characters to your expedition. I love this deck building aspect. The power of the cards you play can be influenced by what people have played around you (to get resources for example) and by how many natives you chose to help you, which essentially acts as a multiplier. This introduces a critical timing element into what you chose to do when. You cannot get too greedy or over zealous when building your deck because characters left over when you build camp work against you (your expedition is too big and unwieldy). 

The bacon strip wrapped around this filet is the worker placement component to this game. Mmmm mmmmm!

Like Power Grid, I did get the sense that once you fell behind it was extremely challenging to catch back up. I could keep pace, but never did I get the sense that I was going to get back in it. To help you out, Power Grid invokes the reverse turn order to give advantages to the people in the back. L&C has some pretty neat "catch-up" character cards that you could utilize to get you back in it.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

western

RATING: 8

Has a good mixture of strategy and tactics, and is quite the novel and clever design, plus no-one can deny the stellar components and artwork. Mind you, this needs at least 1-2 plays to get into the cadence of the game. A fine game.

This is 'Are We There Yet? - the Board Game'. I know this is about a long and arduous trek across North America that required sacrifice and meticulous planning - but does it have to feel like it?
This was a total flop with my group - we gave up after the first mountain range, there was simply no excitement despite its central element supposedly being a race. Based on the high scores from others I can only surmise that this game really appeals to those more inclined towards the 'puzzle' elements of games - those who get real enjoyment from thinking through complex game engines to calculate and plan what they need to do for each of the next ten turns. To me it just feels too linear - while there are certainly different strategies it all boils down to moving a cardboard camp marker along an unhelpfully small journey track. There's just no sense of an epic journey unfolding - or perhaps there is something exciting happening but you've been employed to stay back in the office as the accountant...

RATING: 7

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
10 steps forward, 5 back. 4 steps forward, 6 back. 12 steps forward, loose half of 'em because the correct transport is not available. Switch production to another kind of resource so you can do the forward-backward thing in a different (but in fact arbitrary) region of track. Jump through hoops to make your cards produce two different transports at the same time, curse when you fall back. And still finish first. Despite this giving 'two steps forward and one step back' a whole new meaning, I had this nagging feeling that something felt familiar. It took me a while to work out what: Lewis & Clark is in fact a puzzle game not unlike some of the designs Stefan Feld cranked out of late. Players more or less do whatever they want, see each other only sparingly but are still able to influence each other's game state thusly. That is what Castles of Burgundy and its brethren do, too. But where Stefan attempts to give players something fresh to puzzle with, Cédrick limited himself to very standard aspects: shared worker placement, special action cards, resource conversion. Only the card activation mechanism is novel... but I'm not sure that that is sufficient to keep the game afloat over time. What most certainly [i]does[/i] help is that the game comes with material to alter the road the players take. This changes the way in which players should construct their card playing engines considerably, thus keeping matters fresh. 

Overall, my response to Lewis and Clark is rather 'meh'. It's not because the game doesn't function: in fact it plays perfectly fine. I am also not saying that it is easy to finish first, because I have this nagging suspicion that more experienced players will be over the Rockies before you have struck camp a few meager places beyond the starting location. There is definitely skill in playing efficiently here. But it just won't strike a chord with me... probably because of all the mundane mechanisms it employs. Your mileage may vary.

Oh, one important detail which ought to be mentioned: finally a box with little air inside once everything is stored away.

RATING: 5
complexity without good reason
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
I think the combined action choice / deck building mechanic is very interesting and has enormous potential! :) Which is why I was looking forward to trying out this game.

However, unfortunately I didn't find the details of the implementation particularly convincing... :(
I like complex games in general, but I think this one is too complex without gaining enough strategical depth from this complexity to be really worth it. Our 4 player game had very much downtime between turns.
I expect this would change with more games, but in order to get tolerable, I expect several games and study of the many different cards to be needed. For now, the game didn't really click enough for me that I feel like I really want to invest this time.
There are too many different action cards for my taste - 60 different ones and only one copy of each. I would prefer if instead of the 54 different encountered characters, there would be, say, six copies of 10 different ones. That would both reduce chaotic complexity and increase the possibilites for strategically planned card combos.
As is, the process of buying new characters seems too chaotic and luck based to me.
The characters also don't seem balanced enough. Some are just so much stronger than others (particularly #53, Sacagawea - but others are mighty strong as well) and I think this isn't by far balanced enough by the mere 2 difference in Equipment cost between a strength 1 and a strength 3 card (considering how easy you can get loads of resources even with the abilities of your starting characters after a few turns when there are lots of visible badges).
What really pushed the game over the top for me complexity-wise are the Shamanism action in the village and the character "The Partisan" (35) which also allows to copy opponent's characters (up to 3 times with strength 3). So you even have to consider what all of the opponent's characters would do for you most of the time!

My rating and this comment is based on only one game (with 4 players) without reading the rulebook myself, so you should definitely take it with a grain of salt.

I think it would be best to learn this game with two players or even in a solo game, so it probably wasn't ideal to first try it out with four.
And I guess in order to learn this game, you have to be willing to play several games in which you just wing many things and don't bother to even want to try to play anywhere near optimally - which is difficult for me! ;)

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
This was a great deal of fun, and seems to promise a fair amount of variation from game to game. I'm a real fan of the "slowly self-modifying no-random-draw hand" dynamic this game uses (one of my games currently in development does something similar), and there were enough points of interaction [racetrack, village actions, card drafting, resource generation] to keep it feeling like other players were relevant to my play experience.

There are some lovely pain-points in the route you're racing along - for instance, the first land travel is 7 mountains long, which is more than you can get past with most single card-plays. But if you're first to those mountains (stopping at the joint land/water space), the next player(s) to show up will end up on the space beyond... and you can get over 6 land spaces in a single action with many more cards, including one in your starting expedition! So there's real benefit to either not being first, or if you are first to racing a few spaces into the mountains right off the bat so other expeditions can't benefit from your scouting.

I also like that all of the character cards are apparently real people (or in one case, a real dog) involved in the Lewis & Clark saga. 

I've heard thirdhand mutterings of a card combo which turns the game mostly solitaire, which would be sad, but probably avoidable by just omitting one of them from the deck.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
This is definitely a classic.  It presents us with some of the main things we like in games.  First, there are the multi-use cards: each card is an ability, or a power for another card.  Using it as a power, though, takes it out of use for that round: you won't get it back until you camp.  This gives rise to a number of tough decisions, which interacts with the next thing we like: the market.

Like Concordia, L&C offers 5 cards for purchase at any given time.  Unlike Concordia, all of L&C's cards are unique.  So each game, each round, you are presented with a unique crop of characters to consider.  This gives another level of tough decisions, and the opportunity to "pivot" to a new strategy if someone interesting comes out.  

There's also the temptation to overdo it - to buy too many people and bloat your engine.  If you don't buy enough, though, you won't make much progress along the course.

All in all, this is a fantastic game.  Plus, amazing art from Vincent DuTrait!

RATING: 7
This game is a hearty, complex euro.  One-third of it is worker placement, the other two-thirds are deck-building and hand management.  Both elements constitute ways to take your one mandatory action each turn. 

What's tricky is that, for the most part, you have to burn a card to activate another, producing some brain-burning AP.  And the game's symbology is pretty opaque, at least for the first play.

But there's a pay-off in that the game is very strategic.  There is also some theme, not just mindless VP-accumulation, and some, but not much, player interaction.

RATING: 8
A race game combining deck building and worker placement. Each player starts with the same basic deck (although the historical figures are different) and has to collect resources and workers (indians) in order to obtain means of transport (canoes and horses) and extend his deck with historical figures which each have specific properties as regards the collection of resources and/or the way in which they facilitate transport. In order to get back the cards you've played, you have to make camp. This takes a lot more time if you're overloaded with resources and have a whole lot of workers following you in their canoes. So you really have to balance your belongings with your needs. Travel light, travel fast, but don't forget you can't make it on your own.
Oh yes, did I already mention the theme is the discovery of the western parts of the North American continent? No I didn't, did I. Well, that's because during the game the only discovery you make, is that your strategy to obtain horses to get across those damn mountains sucks big time... The game is not about discovery at all. It's a rather complicated race game. This does not mean it's no fun. It is. There are also a lot of different cards in the game, and this increases replayability.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: N/A

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
I'd love to see more Euro games like this that actually have a victory condition (as opposed to just collecting points throughout the game or adding up points at the end).

To win this game, you have to reach the Pacific Ocean first, but it's not simply a race to a certain number of points. As you travel, portions of the trip are by river and portions are through mountains and you sometimes have to sacrifice progress because of delays setting up camp. The timing of the travel, setting up camp and the transitions between river and mountain and back again make it a challenging journey and not just a race to 100 points.

Added to that are the excellent and interlocking card mechanisms. Cards can be used in two different ways--for their action or to use their level to multiply another card's action. Beyond that, the cards also have resource icons that affect the resources you (and your neighbors) can collect. Many of the cards do interesting and unusual things, not just +1 This and +1 That.

That would all probably be enough to impress me, but there's also a shared worker pool of Native American Indian meeples. The actions these workers can take, and the costs and benefits of having boats to carry Indians and resources, and the ability to use Indians to increase activations of cards, all just work together with everything else here to make a great game.

The biggest downside to the game is that some turns can be very long since you'll sometimes have a ton of planning to do and a myriad of options to consider. (There is serious brainburn available here.)

Another minor quibble is that the game could use a few more resource tokens. They provide some 3X multiplier tiles, but I'd have preferred 5 or 6 more resource tokens, particularly for wood.

Very good game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 9

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
A race down the river to discover the Wild West of the United States of America. Sounds like a great concept for a game. And if you strip Lewis & Clark down to its basics then you have a fairly simple system. Stripped of its veneer you are simply playing a card which either collects resources or converts resources into points (moving up the river). You can spend resources on either buying points (moving up river), or on buying new cards, which mostly simply allow you to use your resources more efficiently and effectively in the future. When you pick your played cards back up ('set up camp') your points take a hit depending on the resources you have and the cards in hand. The first one to get to 40 points (reach the Pacific) on a turn after you have picked your cards up (set up camp) and taken the relevant points deduction is the winner. 
And at its bare bones that is it. Yet, despite this apparent simplicity this makes a great game. Why? Four reasons: 
(1) the theme and the implementation of the theme is good. 
(2) the movement up river has an interesting twist when you hit mountains and a different set of resources are necessary to move. 
(3) thrown into the simple resource to points (movement) mechanics are Indians which do two things: they power up your cards or they can be played into a communal area instead of cards, largely for collecting resources. The former adds to the negative points you will get, the latter means your opponents can then grab the Indians you use. 
And (4) some of the cards and the Indian actions do more than increase the efficiency of the resource conversion, though perhaps there isn't quite enough variety in this area. 
So all in all Lewis & Clarke takes a very basic mechanic, adds a great theme, and then throws a few interesting spanners into the works which add a lot more than glossy veneer. 
I have two criticisms. Firstly it is largely a solitaire experience, the interaction being largely in the competition for cards and the timing of a grab for the communal Indians available. Second criticism is that there is a lot of puzzling and planning required, and on your turn you can do three things (play a card, buy a card, set up camp) and a combination of these two aspects of the game means that downtime with 4 or 5 players is very long. I have heard of 5 player games hitting 4 hours. Far too long for this game. So three will be the sweet spot. Another great game from what is looking like a terrific set of releases from Essen 2013.

RATING: 7
This is a surprisingly crunchy game and I like it.  It didn't quite click for me until it was too late, so by the time the game started making sense, I had no way of catching up.  That being said, I was able to get close as the more experienced players tore away.

A tough mix of hand and resource management means L&C is a game you want to avoid playing with AP-prone people.  As a courtesy, you ought to be planning out your turn well in advance with a contingency in case someone blocks you.

When the board hits the table, the immediate feeling is Stone Age, if only because all of the worker spots are depicted in the same way with the chalk circles - the difference here (and its made very clear) that this isn't exactly a worker placement game.  The meat of the game is rather in gathering the wickedly-cool hex-shaped resource "nuts" in order to facilitate you moving down the river and crossing the mountains as quickly as possible.  Workers (Natives) facilitate this, but the bulk of the game is in using your hand of cards to perform actions.  The neat thing here is that you must sacrifice cards in order to power up the ones you want to use, thereby robbing you of the chance to use the other one until you gather them back up into your hand.  However, doing so too early will actually hinder your progress as each card remaining in your hand when you do the gather action will send your explorer backwards.  It's a very clever way to ensure that players are optimizing the use of all of their cards before daring to pick them back up.  As the game goes on, each player will have opportunities to spend precious resources to either move along the track to victory or buy up better cards.  It's a wonderful decision tree that doesn't feel overwhelming, but again, avoid AP players.

In fact, for any game, avoid AP players.  You people are the friggin worst.

RATING: 8.5
I was thinking I'd immediately fall in love with this game as soon as I played it.  As it happened, after the first few plays I thought it was a solid game but love was not part of the equation.  The cards are all kind of similar and you can kind of see who's going to win three quarters of the way through the game.

As I play it more, I'm really starting to love it.  I merely liked the game before because I didn't really understand all of the options in front of me.  Unimpressive looking cards can be used to crushing effect when used at the right time and/or accompanied by the right actions.  You really have to plan hard in this game and look at all of the options as they unfold.  

You need to have a strategy, but you also need to be open to changing it should better possibilities crop up in the cards that become available.  All that careful thought is rewarded by watching your scout and camp steamroll up the river and past everone else.  One of my favorite games here.

The only drawback of this game is that it requires a good bit of thought so there tends to be a good bit of silence at the table if people are quietly taking their turns.  The art in this game is great though and if players get into their hand of people then there are many laughs to be had.

RATING: 9
Card play is what sets this apart from other German games. Everyone starts with the same hand, but you can fine tune your hand by buying and discarding cards. Similar to TS, you can play a card for either its action or its strength but not both. Every card that you don't play by the time you collect points (i.e. camp) counts as negative points. So just play every card, simple right? Wrong because many cards give you resources, and unused resources count as negative points as well! As in TS, you need to have a plan for how to use each card and when to play it. On your turn you can play a card or place a worker but not both, so you have to factor that into your plans as well. The path of river and mountain spaces is really just a scoring track, yet it manages to evoke the racing theme well. 1-3

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Basically there are 7 types of resources to manage: wood (typically used for upgrading your resource/Indian capacity), fur (typically for card acquisition), equipment (also for card acquisition), food (typically for movement along the river), canoes (more efficient movement along the river), horses (movement along the mountains), and Indians (playing cards, strengthening card effects, and board play).  Besides the unique score track, I find it interesting that you can navigate resource acquisition through card play and through the board.  There are many things to consider between the card play, the board play, the score track, and how your resource management affects the score track.  There are aspects of this game that I didn't even try in my first game: I didn't cull a single card from my deck, I didn't use the space on the board that allows you to copy card effects, and I didn't purge the card acquisition track once.  There is definitely a lot of space to explore (pun intended).  Beyond the mechanisms of this game, the game is not only gorgeous but also has a really nice historical flair.  I hadn't even heard of Lewis and Clark and if it wasn't for reading about the game on BGG, if someone had talked to me about the game, I probably would have thought Lois & Clark, the new adventures of superman.  The rule book not only talks about the expedition, but also gives a card-by-card historical blurb about every character card.  Even more impressive is that every players starting characters which are mechanically identical, have unique names, illustrations, and historical information.

This actually has, in my opinion, a fair bit in common with Concordia.  I say this because there is a card market that escalates in cost and when you purchase from it the card goes to your hand.  In both games you will play cards down and at a time of your choosing (up until you no longer have a choice) you can get all of your played cards back.  Also, in each game, your turn is structured in such a way that you play a card from your hand (you have extra options in L&C though).  Concordia and Lewis and Clark both have limited space for resources (well, L&C doesn't have a hard limit but punishes you for having more than necessary). There are more similarities than that, but what makes them different? Concordia marries the victory conditions to cards in your deck with a spatial map and unique resource acquisition. I think of L&C's race track as a VP track with rivers spaces being one type of VP and mountain spaces being another.  L&C marries unique victory conditions to some cards with a worker placement mechanism on the board.    

I do have a minor quibble that has to be mentioned.  The game sometimes feels very... gamey.  In some respects anyway.  Before I give an example, I'll just say that having too many resources on your player board and having cards in your hand can impede your victory condition.  So, you can play cards and not do anything with them. In regards resources, the game rules specifically say you can't ever dump resources unless you go above capacity but you can take less than you need anytime you would acquire resources. So, you can also place Indians on the board, block certain spaces, and not do anything with it.  Actually there are 2 space on the board that allow you to place 1-3 Indians at a time and these spaces have unlimited capacity (ie you can't block the spaces).  You can do this to trigger the space 3 times, but it feels gamey to place 3 there at a time and not do anything in the space simply because I have too many Indians in my expedition.  Also, in one of the two previously mentioned spaces you may discard 3 different types of resources (including a horse) to produce one horse and you can do this a number of times equal to the 1-3 Indians you place there.  Oh look, I have too many non-Indian resources and Indians with having to set up camp soon because my cards are used up. I guess I'll send 3 Indians to the horse space.  I'll activate it two of my three times because I don't have enough resources to do it thrice but I still want to get rid of an Indian. So, I'll trade a horse, a fur and a piece wood for one horse and then 1 piece of equipment, another fur and another horse for a horse.  That just feels incredibly gamey to me.  I'm more of a fan of mechanisms compared to theme though, so I can put it out of my head.  I just thought I would mention it because it's wonky.

If you like any of the following, don't hesitate to at least try this game: worker placement, resource management, card combos, racing and hand management. This game shouldn't be played with 4 or 5 players, because it overstays its welcome. I would say it's best to learn it with 2, to keep the time down. I personally recommend Concordia over this when you consider the ease of play and various maps that make all player counts enjoyable.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Despite being a hodgepodge of mechanics, Lewis & Clark manages to combine its small elements of deck building, worker placement, and resource management combine to create satisfying decisions that invoke the stresses of leading an expedition. The most notable aspect of this game is its representation of time and emphasis on pace; the larger your corps, and the more resources you stock, the longer your journey. This essence of time permeates through every game decision and forces players to establish an optimal tempo for their corps. Adding members to the corps increases one's flexibility, but slows down their deck cycle, and stocking up on resources when they are most prevalent, might save on future actions, but hampers forward progress. This manifestation of time, creates a powerful 'two steps forward one step back' cadence that can doom the a corps if not embraced in their plans. This element of time has proven difficult for new players to embrace, and games often linger longer than desired, but there is enough explore in Lewis & Clark to merit multiple expeditions.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
This is a game for people (like me) who like to combine bonus powers in a race to build and an engine in order to ... in this case, literally race. And while I like this kind of game in general, I particularly like L&C because I find every aspect of the design tight and relevant. 

The worker placement element feels fresh thanks to the way workers are used in the game and the way resources are collected. Workers may be gained or lost via placing on board (vs using on your private action spaces) And you can't easily horde resources -- or workers -- because you're punished for how much stuff and workers you have when you camp. 

And this is where the cards come in; the heart of the game. The point of this game is identifying the cards that will help you navigate the tight worker and resource restrictions the game places on you. Targeting the ones you want and finding combos that work well together -- and being able to buy them before your opponents -- is a large part of this game's DNA. If the card choices are anything but a delight to consider, then I'd say this isn't a game for you.

All in all, I find the creativity required here a delight with how you use and lose your workers and the cards with which you build your engine. I particularly like how everybody ends up doing their own thing. Some save up for big moves, others are happy to go a few spaces at a time. Some shoot up to the mountains and sit while others catch up. 

The game comes with chits to vary the terrain that in theory sound great but we're nowhere near ready for any variation. Lastly, the game also plays solitaire which I'v found to be a little less interesting than I'd hoped but still a worthwhile training ground.


***RULE CLARIFICATIONS***
1) You cannot send 1 Indian on the horse spot and get rid of 2 resources, you need a 3rd different resource to be able to do it. If you don't have any, you can send the indian for nothing, without getting rid of any resource.

2) You  may send Indians to, say, the canoe spot on the board with no wood just to get rid of them (still an action, of course).

3) When your boats are all full, if you collect new resources, you have the choice: either you discard the new reources you have just got, or you get rid of some of the old resources you already have on your boats, in order to make room for the new ones.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
It is good to see that there is still room for innovation in worker placement games although worker placement certainly isn't the primary focus of Lewis & Clark. Hand management is probably a better fit but again the game tweaks the well-known mechanism slightly to create something very different. The real game changer, however, is the punishment for having hoarded too many resources at the end of the turn. In this case, more is most definitely not always better.

Agonizing choices, delicate timing question and vastly diverging strategies, sounds like a recipe for success. There are four niggles that dampen the enthusiasm a little bit, however. Firstly, the multitude of choices offers ample opportunities for AP to set in. Choose wisely who you play with. Secondly, the winner-takes-it-all race aspect is something I'm not particularly fond of but that's simply personal preference. Arguably, neither of these is really a fault with the game. And lastly, the gameplay is a little solitaire-ish. A bit more interaction would be lovely.

What I am most disappointed of, however, is that hanging back in the beginning and stocking up without regards to your load seems to be such a strong approach. It kind of kills a lot of the appeal of the game, trying to inch forward without carrying too much of a burden with you and at the same time stops much of the leeching and thus a large part of what little interactivity there is. I don't want to go as far as to say it's a game breaker, but it has certainly cost the game a rating point or two with me.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8
Lewis & Clark is a very good worker placement/hand building game for 1 to 5 player.  The goal of the game is to be the first player to travel from St. Louis to Fort Clatsop.
This game have a lot of replay value because the cards that are available to take will be different every game.  For even more diversity, you can also use tokens to change some spaces on the board from river to mountain or vice versa.  The solo mode is good to learn how to play but this game is at its best with 3 to 5 player.

RATING: 10
This is great game if you enjoy engine building and history. It can be a pretty long game and the teaching curve can be steep, but once you go through a couple of turns you should be good to go. One of the reasons it can be long is because you have many choices on each turn and your best option may change depending on what action the previous player took. There will also be lots of rulebook reading when it comes to all the various cards but once you understand their icons that too will become easy. This game does not make it to the table as much as I would like because it is a heavier game and takes the right mix of players. It works well with two or more players, but the more players you have the more downtime between turns. More players also means more resources so the game actually moves quicker (more resources, more cards to buy, more actions to take, etc.)

RATING: 7
(Only played once, 5-player)

What I liked most and felt (to me) very innovative was the a-simultaneous gameplay, from the point of the first player setting up camp onward. That's when players' tableaus start diverging: others may have nearly all their cards out, while your tableau is empty after just having set up camp. This has an interesting effect on your decision making and also indicates how your chosen path in this game is different from everybody else's. 

Understanding the cards laid out for purchase was hard. Many were not intuitive in their iconography and we had to reference the rulebook constantly. And for that, I find, there were too many visible at one time.  

Also a 5-player game takes too long. A full game night. Nearly 4 hours! (Admittedly we were all new to the game.)

RATING: 7

RATING: 5
Essen'13
Eurogame de col·locació de treballadors i gestió de mà on guanya el primer en arribar a una determinada casella. Les cartes es poden fer servir de diferents maneres i el joc penalitza l'acumulació de recursos i el "tortugueo".

Té un mecanisme tipus deckbuilding molt interessant, has d'anar millorant la teva mà mitjançant noves cartes d'un stock però sense conservar les que ja no et son útils, ja que això et penalitza. D'aquesta manera sembla que s'evita el atzar en el robament de cartes aleatoriament de una baralla i dona més possibilitats de planificació.

Té moltíssimes possibles estrategies i la rejugabilitat ve donada per l'ordre en el que apareixen les cartes (a més de poder variar el recorregut mitjançant tokens de terreny).

Actualització després de més partides: tot i el nombre elevat de cartes, després de unes quantes partides es repeteix sempre l'estrategia a seguir per guanyar la partida, agafant només un parell o tres de cartes necesaries i deixant pasar la resta. Més repetitiu del que semblava en un principi...

RATING: 6.5
The game has a lot of interesting mechanisms. I want to like the game, but it disappoints me. It boils down to getting the best engine first (most move for least number of actions) to win. Randomness when drawing the available cards to buy can prevent all but one player from getting the "good" cards.
Also, The simple engines seem win over the complex ones, since they take less time (actions) to acquire and to execute. This sort of makes the engine building a bit dull.

Once a player has a good engine going, the lack of player interaction and catch-up mechanisms causes a runaway leader problem.Who will win is often apparent after about half of the game has been played. This sort of kills any tension.
The game is too long for what it offers with 4+ players.

RATING: 8
Last Played: 2010-2015
Plays: 1
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.8
1.起始設置:
印地安人數量:3/4/5(12/15/18) ，1隻放到中央印地安人補充區。 
每人6基本腳色卡。
=>卡片介紹 正面包含: 花費、能力、提供資源、數字(力量值)。 背面:力量直、雇用折扣、提供資源、紮營時間。
抽5張卡依照數字排序，數字最小靠近墨西哥灣。

起始資源: (基本版)1毛皮1食物1裝備，(進階)每種資源各4個，隨機每人拿3個。偵查員及營地放到起始區。

進行:
1.行動(強制)
a.使用村莊能力: 1個印地安人放到空的圈圈，或只1-3個到半圈。
b.使用角色能力: 打出一張卡正面朝上，另一張卡當作力量值背面啟動，可以使用印地安人啟動。
2.紮營(選擇) 行動前或行動後
a.角色卡的印地安人回到探險船上
b.計算角色卡及探險隊的船及印地安人花費。
注意:紮營時間標記旁邊有問號表示每一個資源花費一個時間。
         有印地安人表示每一個印第安人花一格時間。
根據花費時間退後偵查員，若偵查員前進比營地遠將營地帶到偵查員所在地，收回所有卡片。(營地不會後退，偵查員同一格上有人必須多退或多進一格，看前進方向。)
3.雇員(選擇行動)
a.支付其卡片上對應裝備以及角色所在位置的毛皮花費。可以棄掉手中角色卡來支付裝備花費，折抵數量與角色力量值相同。

卡片介紹:
1.口譯員:集結印地安人，棄掉最後一張僱員區的牌

RATING: 9.6
The race game that you might forget is a race game.

You have a hand of cards that lets you do different actions, mostly centered around collecting different types of resources or moving along the expedition track. You can also buy cards that have different or improved versions of the starting cards. One thing nice is you'll rarely see all the cards in the deck (and of course the order is completely random) so you'll have to something different each game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
Essentially a race game but with an innovative combination of worker placement and deck building / hand management driving progress. 

This is a beautiful game from an aesthetic point of view, it's satisfyingly thinky, presents plenty of choices and gets bonus points for its simple but effective solitaire option.

On the downside, I didn't find the game particularly intuitive for the first couple of plays (though it certainly played smoothly enough thereafter) and I found it somewhat harder to teach than other games of similar weight.

RATING: 9
Purchased at Essen 2013
-----
Played a 2p game. Really enjoyed it! It was very close the entire game...I was about 1-2 turns away from finishing it. Got stuck in the last mountain range right at the end.

The "deck building" element is so much nicer than a lot of the other board games with deck building (ie Mage Knight, Legendary, etc) because you get the card you recruit immediately. There is no hoping you will be able to draw it with the other cards that work together. Being able to expand your player area to told more Indians and resources is also great. All around a very nice game which I hope to play with more players.

RATING: 7
Juego de gestión de recursos que incluye una mecánica de gestión de mano curiosa.

Se trata de un juego ambientado en la exploración del norte de América en la época un pelín anterior al "lejano oeste". Cada jugador pilota una tripulación que tendrá que hacer avanzar su expedición en una carrera por llegar el primero a un punto.

La gracieta del juego está en ir adquiriendo una serie de personajes (cartas) que permitirán por un lado realizar acciones y por otro lado pagarlas, acompañándolas de nuestros indios (kekos)

Tanto los kekos como los recursos los meteremos en nuestras canoas, que son limitadas y presentan un coste adicional de cara a establecer el campamento (que permite recuperar las cartas jugadas).

Gráficamente muy vistoso

RATING: 8.5
Really cool race game with a bit of an engine building feel. But you can only build up so much since you really need to use as many of your resources as possible before you rest and get your cards back to use. I love this aspect of the game, that requires fairly precise planning to make sure you have enough, but not too many resources. It's also really cool how you can only use about half of your cards between rests and you can power them up with the same guys you use for the worker placement. The worker placement itself is pretty interesting in how it can sort of round out what your cards can't do, besides being the only way to get more storage space. And you have to be really careful how you gain and lose workers just like resources. I also really like the hand building aspect, that mechanic is quickly becoming one of my favorites. And you really do need to find some good way of getting over mountains, because the way in your starting deck won't cut it.

RATING: 5.5
Some good ideas and great theme, but ultimately too complicated for the limited time and scope of the game--get to the end of the board. It's a gamer's game in rules and concept overhead but with a kind of samey--turn goods into better goods, optimize your deck play feel.

After a first game of "whaaaa???" you will have an appreciation for how everything works--it is consistent and logical. I would be a lot more comfortable the next time I played and would likely play a lot better also. But I'm not inclined to do so.

The Indians, the boats, the camping, the huge array of characters integrate well with each other and the theme as a whole. Pretty and functional map, counters and cards. Beautiful. The history is fantastic.

There seems to be a high degree of luck regarding getting mountain transportation. Someone can luck into a character that provides an efficient means for mountain crossing. I'm guessing among players of relatively equal strength, that's huge.

I was in the minority of people I talked to at the convention where I played this--most loved it.

RATING: 5
2014-04-18

Initial Rating: 6.0 (April 2014)

As I dislike racing games (fortunately Lewis & Clarke doesn’t feel like one :D), I wasn’t expecting much from Lewis & Clarke. At first it surprised me with the game mechanics, but then I think there is a luck and a relevance issue with the cards so I’m not sure what to think of the game.

On the good side, love the decisions that arise when playing cards (which card should I keep, sacrifice or play), great art and components, very novel (for me at least) back tracing mechanism (you need to plan it carefully or lost many spaces). In short, I love the game play. 

On the bad side, I dislike that players can’t share the same spot as many times players surpassed or delayed other players by luck (not planning), also it seems unfair that a player can get ahead just by playing next in turn order. The first player has a disadvantage with the resources (as his neighbors don’t have any played cards). As with any variable card offering, I feel there is a lot of uncontrollable luck (unbalanced cards) when a good card appears and thus when the current player can buy it (I heavily dislike this), moreover in our games there was no need to really buy cards, with the ones in your hand and perhaps another previously bought card you can compete and do well. This led me to think that the cards are not that important in the game. With the same reasoning (but with less severity), the players who bought the additional expedition tiles did worse as they spent resources in tiles, not advancing. I’m not sure they are relevant or not (I played the basic game, perhaps with the custom map my card and expedition tiles issue can disappear) and I only thought of them as another way to get a resource, not for its ability to get more room with less time penalties. The game can be long. The resources can be very easy to obtain due to the multipliers, due to this there is no tension.

The theme don’t do anything for me, if anything I found it boring HOWEVER, it fits perfectly with the game mechanics. I think this is best with 3 players, with more the downtime begins to be more than I like. 

In conclusion, Lewis & Clarke is a good game. I feel it has uncontrollable luck with the cards and (paradoxically) I think the cards are not very relevant to the game. In short the deckbuilding aspect is pure tactics and thus flawed (for me). I need to explore it further to see if the card issue is real or imaginary. For now, I won’t buy the game.

Current Rating: 6.0

RATING: N/A
Never played this with other folks, so I can't officially rate it. Two guys in my game group have played it and have no interest in playing it again, so I'm unlikely to get it to the table soon. Trading my copy.

I did get in a learning game playing three-handed and played it to the end. This is a resource management racing game leveraging both worker placement and deck-building (actually hand-building) to get what you need. The workers are shared among all players. Once some workers are placed, as a card action a player can claim all played workers. Advancing on the track is a two step process. Players use food, canoes and horses advance their scouts and then players can choose to move their camp up to their scouts, but the scouts lose ground for every card still in hand and having too many resources or workers. The game is about balancing the advancement of your scout while using up all of your cards, resources and workers before camping. 

While I think the game is an interesting mix of mechanics and game styles, none of card or worker actions in themselves are all that interesting. The game is in the balancing act to move your camp forward. I can see, though, that each game could start to feel a bit samey after a while and I'm not sure that timing your encampments is a strong enough draw to keep coming back. I can see why my friends can very well take or leave this game. I could see myself in the same boat, as well. This game falls squarely between a 6 and a 7 for me, and those games usually end up a 6.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 6

Not bad. Not great. I wish it wasn't so darn long. Both of my games have gone 3+ hours.

RATING: 8
Probably the best of the 2013 Essen crop.  Takes the deck-building concept made popular in Dominion to a race game.  Multiple paths to victory given what bonus cards to draft, and you also have to decide to go for a big expedition (with a slower rate of turnover and possible movement penalties) versus a "lean and mean" approach that avoids penalties when you camp but also may not have the right combination of drafted cards to compete with the bigger deck, and then finally there is the issue of how to play the Indian recruiting and usage aspect of the game.  Highly innovative approach to the subject, and also enjoy the historical information in the game -- similar to Founding Fathers, another favorite of mine.

RATING: 7
I like the engine building. You have to carefully craft your hand to efficiently move up the river and over the mountains. Finding a mountain movement card seems very important, or, failing that, a better source of horses. The decisions you have to make to plan out your turns are interesting and at times tense. 

This game, like most race games, is prone to runaway leader issues. The end game can be absolutely boring as the winner is clear several turns in advance. Even if the win is hotly contested in a 3-4p it is likely contested between only two players. Thus players are essentially eliminated, although they get to keep taking turns.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8

Components are mostly top-notch though board re-print for my edition is smaller and gets a little cramped. Also does not include resource stickers included in previous printing. Runaway leader can be a problem, as in most racing games. For all its flaws, though, it’s SO clever.

RATING: 8
---------------------------------------
[b]My thoughts on Lewis & Clark[/b]
Lewis & Clark is an innovative hybrid of a worker placement game with a card combo hand management game, in which you'll take the role of an exploration party that tries to discover and cross the US from the east to the west coast, just like the historical figures Lewis and Clark did.
So far, I have only played this game solo, but I can't wait to explore it with more players. I really appreciate the mechanics, I love the fact that it is essentially a racing game with a fixed game ending, and the artwork is gorgeous.

[b]Star ranking[/b]
Fun: :star::star::star::star::nostar:
Satisfaction: :star::star::star::star::nostar: 
Esthetics: :star::star::star::star::star:
Replayability: :star::star::star::star::star: 
[b]Verdict: Still exploring, but probably a keeper[/b]
---------------------------------------
March 2015: Sold off
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
A unique race game driven by building an engine out of 54 unique cards that can be bought from the display. As there's only a subset of 5 cards available at a time, players will be forced into different strategies at a time. The worker placement part luckily is only a necessary but not central addition. More decisive is clever hand management and synergistic deckbuilding.
Lewis & Clark invites to play with gorgeous artwork. The gameplay is unexpectedly thinky each move and will cause AP. Nothing for players averse to solving puzzles or solitairish games. 
For me, the result is satisfying enough to keep the promise made. My only little niggle after one game is the potentially overpowered strategy of staying behind until the engine is fully build up and then making a dash to the goal with only two or three intermediate stops. Hopefully, strategies more in the sense of the theme can be as competitive.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Race game with a economic engine.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Had fun playing this one with my mom, a Lewis and Clark buff. At its heart it is worker placement. Calling it a racing game is a stretch. It plays out like a sort of Pillars of the Earth with cards. Unfortunately, the way that you collect resources in the game feels strange and doesn't fit the theme (how am I getting resources from other player's party members?) It also really slows down the game, while adding a level of strategy that many players enjoy. All in all, I like the game a lot, reflected by my rating. The negatives I mentioned just stick from such an otherwise great game. 

On a different note, my mom did not like the way that indians are used like a sort of currency/resource in the game. I thought that it was thematic and worked well.

Update: This game feels so different than many euros in the immediate tension you feel in planning your hands so you do not lose time when you set up camp. That is balanced by the long term strategy of drafting cards to make your engine work. It feels thematic in the specific actions you take as well as in the people (historically based) you use to take those actions. Great game!
