RATING: 5
there is a lot to like about this game such as cool mechanics and compelling gameplay, but there is too much action paralysis for my taste. you have to meticulously plan out your moves to get the maximum efficiency possible, but you can't really do this when it isn't your turn because what other people do on their turns affects your decisions. this means there is a lot of downtime between your turns as you have to wait for each other player to figure out their best possible move.

also, once you get behind there is no catching up. this is fine in a game like dominion that only takes 30 minutes to play, but when a game lasts 2-3 hours it's completely unacceptable.

RATING: 6
Starts very promisingly, but as soon as the thematically important mountains kick in the game slows down too much. The mountains only add anything through theme; besides that they don't give this game really anything. The game might look like a racing game, but it's really a hand building game with a big resemblance to Martin Wallace's London: you get a handful of cards which you try to play as effectively as possible after which you camp your expedition and get penalty in a way like in London. I prefer London to this first and foremost because in London players are in control of the game's clock and it's not so easy to see who's the winner some rounds before the end.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
First, L&C is a hoot and a half to play: the mixture of deckbuilding and worker placement works great together, giving you different avenues towards the win. Second, I believe this is a piece of art of design: so many small details have been twisted to make this a simple but deep game. It doesn't have the thousands of counter-examples that many other games do, and make it a hassle to remember.

All in all, it's a tight game with great diversity.

RATING: 5
Feels refreshing and after couple of first rounds you have got the whole idea here. Long game, but luckily very little downtime. I would give it 7 at least, but two quite major problems: 1) you can build your engine at first and forget all the damage because you can't go any backward on river - after that start pushing forward quickly and win the game... boring, but effective strategy. 2) the river/mountain spaces in the end part are bit problematic, because every player get stuck in a same part of river. I would really like to see this game engine used somewhere again, because it works.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: N/A
Lewis & Clark features dual use cards. To be activated, one card must be combined with another one, which becomes unavailable for a while. Thus, players are faced with a constant dilemma: play a card or sacrifice it. During the game, each player acquires character cards that enlarge his hand, building a crew that gives him more options but it needs to be optimized as he will recycle his cards more slowly. This new "handbuilding" mechanism fits strongly the historical background.

RATING: 7.5
Preliminary rating; I'm still thinking about what to think.

This is a mixture of every gamer sweetheart from the past few years.
Worker placement combined with somewhat Dominion-like deck-building, 7 Wonders-like resource collection, RftG-like multiple use cards, all built on a kind of race game...
Actually it's quite good, but it's very fiddly, kind of the opposite of elegant. Also, don't play it with AP players as they make the game go too slow. It should not go slow! It's a race, people! Listen to your instincts!

RATING: 7
At it's heart, it's a racing (expedition) game with deck(hand)-building, hand management, resource management and worker placement.  

You don't want your deck/hand to be extensive (5 or 6 cards seems ideal) b/c then it takes you many turns to cycle through (play) all cards in order to setup camp.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Initial impression is very positive as there appears to be quite a bit of difficult decision making as well as an interesting blend of deck-building, worker placement, and resource management.  Deck building occurs as you hire additional workers which generally enhance your strategy/actions and also provide higher strength support.  Worker placement is a bit unique in that the Indians are for everyone and if you use them in the village instead of your expedition, someone else could acquire their help.  Instead of playing a card, you can take an action in the Indian village.  In addition, there is a fair bit of resource management as you must acquire a number of resources and face lost time in this racing game if you maintain large stores of resources on your boats.  

Artwork is bright and colorful.  Card stock may be a bit flimsy, but I sleeve my cards in most cases anyway.  For PC reasons, the publisher might have been better served to avoid using the color red for the Indian meeples.

NEVER EVER play a teaching game with five...even if you have played before and know the rules.  Takes too freaking long!  I doubt I will play this one again with AP prone people; I don't handle the waiting well as they go through all their possibilities.

RATING: 6.5
I won, but I don't exactly know how. Maybe it's because I tried to stay lean, with not too many boats in my convoy, and rarely too much load.

S was left behind because of a lack of Indians, of too many useless cards in her hand, that set her back whenever she moved a bit, or gave her too many resources. This was frustrating. This is a flaw in the game, I think: people should never feel like they're just going through the moves with no hope.

The fight for the first position was nice.

The special powers are a little too uniform (a lot of get food/wood/fur/…), and purchasing too many cards is a bit of a pain (they set us back at the end) so we don't have too many special powers each anyway.

It's nice to each have different cards, though. The progression in terms of what we can do is nice, too (4 basic cards at the beginning, maybe 7–10 cards at the end).

It's a nice euro. I enjoyed it more than Terra Mystica, with its victory point soup that muddled the impact of our actions: at least in Lewis & Clark the race element makes us want to "move forward" instead of trying to get more points, even though the two are similar. There is more of an engine element to Lewis & Clark than in Terra Mystica, I found, which means that we learn to move fast instead of trying to opportunistically grabbing points here and there.

RATING: 5
There's stuff I should have liked about this. I like the theme. Artwork isn't bad, though there's an inexplicable use of the infinity symbol on some cards and the usual Euroglyphics are annoying. I like that they used real people (including a dog!) and you can read about them in the rulebook. The red Native Americans are a sadly unfortunate choice, but I do like that the game emphasized just how much help the native population was. 

But good god this game is boring. Just the same thing, over and over, the same Agricola-like thirty-steps-to-get-a-horse bullshit repeated ad nauseum. Get one AP player in there and you better bring a book. It's tedious, the promising theme is under-utilized, and you can almost pinpoint the exact space on the board where you stop caring.

RATING: 8
Finally a game that follows the same vein as 51st State with multi-function cards. It's more of a race than anything, and exhillerating until the end.

There's an interesting counterpoint between the hand management and resource management aspects of the game. You want to rotate through your hand as fast as you can but when you do you also get penalized for carrying over too many resources and Indians. You might try and reduce your resource and Indian load but then each card becomes less efficient. 

One thing is for certain: No two players will play the game the same, at least not until after MANY plays. 

I really love the Dutrait art as well.

RATING: 7.5
[microbadge=13167]

This was pitched to me as a deck-builder, which it isn't.  It's a hand-builder, since you always have access to all of your unplayed cards, and then you get all of your played cards back to your hand at the same time.  Odd racing game mostly based around changing cubes into different cubes.  The whole "check your neighbors" mechanic added some interesting interaction, but that's pretty much the only interaction, apart from some light worker placement and drafting denial.  Interesting execution of theme.

RATING: 9.5
This game is a blast! Theme an mechanics are perfectly presented. The components are top notch: original indian figures, ›special‹ resource tokens, nice and clear iconography, beautiful board and absolute great cards - nice and clear. 

This game has its own unique feel and flow. Sometimes it is a brainer, sometimes it is ›obvious‹ what to do. There are so much decisions to make and there are always multiple options for a good turn. That said, you can bring yourself in a, let me say, very unprofitable situation as well. Always remember it is a race. Do not spend all your effort to build a perfect expedition for future turns - do the great progress just in time. And don´t forget to watch your opponents.

Lewis&Clark scales very well from 1-5 players (i can imagine that 5 players could a bit long and sometimes you have to wait quit long. especially if somebody is prone to ap)

With its solo-variant and the option to change the racetrack with the included river and mountain tokens the replayability is tremendous. Not to forget that every play there are other cards and therefore other card combinations available.

Maeddes likes this game very very much :)

RATING: 8
Love this game.  Great efficiency exercise, lots of good card combos to try out for different strategies.  Enjoy the resource-gathering-from-your-neighbor mechanic (similar to parts of 7 wonders).  Gorgeous artwork, historical tidbits in the rulebook are greatly appreciated and enhance the theme (which was already quite interesting). Don't know why everyone complains about runaway leader issues; so far all the games I've played have had second place only behind by a maximum of three turns, which to me isn't that long.

Can be a tad long when learning.  I think the length comes mostly from AP, which definitely can be an issue, since there are quite a few different things you could do with your turn, and you want to try and plan out each movement/camp cycle perfectly.  However, once everyone knows the game, not so bad at all.

Overall, thoroughly enjoyable, can see me playing this one a lot more in the future.

A játék tele van egyedi mechanizmusokkal (táborozás, indiánok, toborzás), a témáját is tökéletesen ráhúzták a vázra, az összes karakterkártya létező személyről van formázva, tehát minden adott lenne a (közel) tökéletes játékhoz, de valahogy mégsem.

RATING: 9
SOLO CAPABLE

120 Min.

Amazing worker placement game. Works really well as a solitaire game which is a huge plus. Great flow, great theme integration… easy to learn, gorgeous components… tons of variety with the various role cards - can't wait to explore different strategic paths. Feels about as meaty as an Uwe Rosenberg game, but in certain ways the flow is better. It's early, but for now this baby is an enthusiastic 10. Just a great game all around. Some have issues with it being a "race" game - frankly, I prefer it to Agricola's complicated end-game scoring. As for solo - the difficulty is easily scalable by trying to beat the game in X amount of moves. 

Update: Pulled it out after not touching it for a while - totally holds up.

RATING: 9.1
I love Lewis and Clark.  There are so many interesting and novel aspects to the game.  Even resource collection requires players to think ahead and plan carefully - as you can often take a large quantity of resources, but taking more than you can use will later result in penalties.  When choosing actions, cards have to be played in pairs, so you need to carefully consider what you will be doing in your next few turns each time you play. More than any other game I've played, this game requires precise future-planning.

RATING: 5
PROS:
- components, neat meeples

- the card illustrations

- a few neat mechanisms (eg. all workers are neutral, count well how much do you need) and interesting ideas in the card deck

- The game really punishes you for counting things wrong, you lose the position on the "victory river" for anything surplus you had in the moment of recycling your deck. This is what I consider a good thing, euros in general should reward optimization and good thinking.

CONS:
- The overall theme doesn't sell it for me. Some might like the game precisely for the theme, though.

- The among-player interaction (traditionally lower in euros) is not high here, too.  I wouldn't mind that... but generally the player interaction feels very random. I seriously doubt with all my hand management I have to do anyway, I would still have some brain capacity to watch which neighbors-affecting symbols to use and which ones to avoid... One card just has too many  various effects. 

- Every action you plan, every movement on the "victory point river" takes SEVERAL TURRRRNS to launch. Everything here feels slow, especially the deck building.

The last two "cons" might gradually disappear with more plays, but at the moment I'm not rushing into that... :(

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
Really enjoy this one.  Even after the first game, I started to see there's a variety of possible strategies, and they depend heavily on the character cards that become available -- which differ from game-to-game.

After playing a game for the first time, my wife & I frequently enjoy discussing what other games the new game felt like. For L&C, we said it felt like:

- Dominion, because you are building your 'deck' with new characters... but not exactly, because unlike in Dominion, you can choose exactly when you want to play your cards rather than the cards coming up randomly.
- Stone Age, because there's a worker placement element and the board art is very nice... but not exactly, because worker placement is only a portion of L&C, while it's the entire game in Stone Age.
- Through the Ages: A Story of Civilization, because there's that familiar mechanic of new cards becoming available to buy, and sliding down & becoming cheaper over time.

But what's different about L&C from most of our other games is that it's a race game! I really like the fact that it's a race -- and not a victory point collection game, as is the case with most of our games.

RATING: 6
Lewis & Clark is a race game where card management is important. Those are typically two things that turn me off but this game adds some interesting restrictions. Knowledge of and luck with available cards may also be too important, as some seem more powerful than others. Nevertheless, there is enough in this game to create a tense and entertaining gameplay.

RATING: 8
This is one of the best game in Essen 2013. 

The interesting part of the game is you have a lot to decide while the game play is easy. This openness awards creative players that can plan and make a sequence of plays best to move forward. The one-dimensional goal - be the first to reach the destination also makes it much more elegant than recent euros that awards so many endgame bonuses.

The sheer amount of combos also make it very interesting to explore the game mechanisms. The interaction between players is very subtle, and that is a pretty good thing in this type of game - you have enough to consider just with your own cards, so passive interaction is more than enough.

The only downside is having to check the symbols on the cards often (they aren't too easy to figure out) and some players can get AP as it requires a lot of future plannings.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
Players are racing from St Louis to get to the Pacific Ocean first. During your turn, your mandatory action includes taking a chracter card action or taking an action in the village, which requires Indians. Optionally, you can recruit more characters or set up camp, which may pull you backwards, but allows you to get all your cards again to use. 

The game has some interesting ideas and you want to create some card combinations that work. The trouble is, setting up camp on the first turn is the only way to play the game strategically. If you don't set camp, you leave your symbols on the table for other people to take advantage, and they will pass you standing still. If you persist in leaving your symbols on the table, your options for plays get weaker, and you might as well set camp anyways. So to be successful, the game becomes an exercise in getting ready for the big push, and limits the variety. Sure there have been a few good suggestions on how to prevent the first turn camp set up, such as adding mountains behind St. Louis. 

Some cards are obviously more powerful than others, and where they show up in the deck does result in some luck. 

The game could probably use a few tweaks to be a good game.

Note: On mid-August 2014, the designer and publisher added a new rule to prevent one from setting camp and falling 5 spaces behind (and no one can use your symbols, while you get to re-use your cards). The rule makes you discard all cards but your starting hand, and limits your resources. It seems like a good rule, and a fix should have been implemented a long time ago, but at least the fix is now part of the official rules. I'm not sure if this will bring the game out of the graveyard for me, but I am willing to try it again. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Tricky to wrap your head around, with the multiple uses for cards, the use of Indians, the need for a lean expedition, and the desire to actually make progress up the river.  Not easy to play, with meaningful decisions and trade-offs at every step, but very rewarding.
The game rapidly becomes a monster with more players, as the 30 minutes-per-player becomes a 2h+ marathon.  In contrast the included solo rules make a delightful solo puzzle, while the highly recommended fan-supplied [filepage=143838]AutoMacKenzie[/filepage] variant adds variety and tension similar to a multiplayer experience.

[i]I have only played this once and initially my thoughts on the game were higher. Now that I look back on the experience I am not sure how often I would want to play this game. I love race games with card play [i](RFtG is a top 5 game for me)[/i], but I want a clean streamlined experience.

You really need to focus on the cards you get to help you become more efficient with your actions and ignore the allure of the board spaces; then my question becomes why bother including all of the WP elements in this game, it just slows the play way down.[/i]

RATING: N/A
Excellent game with a wonderful compilation of historical figures that help you out on your travels! I believe this game is a gateway or medium weight game at heart. But a lot of thought has to go into preparing your band of boats to make their journey up the river and mountains. So because of this, games tend to last a good while, even with just 2 players. The mechanics are really very innovative and fun to work out as you play. 

In this game you have a hand of cards which every turn you will take one to use for it's action and another of your cards to use for it's Native American workers. You also will have the option to recruit other Indians to  assist in your actions. Turns are very simple. You play a card and have the option to recruit another historical figure to help you on your journey (a card to add to your hand). Your cards are your most powerful options every turn, but you do have the option to send your Indian helpers to town to get goods and services for you. 

The game is essentially a race to the Pacific coast. The first to cross the flag space wins. The combined use of your collection of cards and the worker placement part of the game really make this game unique and fun to play. It is strategic, but so simple that I feel many new players can pick it up quickly and play easily. Especially since the rule book is so well organized with examples and it is written very well.

RATING: N/A
2/14,
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
I played this mostly on a whim, hoping for something "not awful". So the fact that this is really quite an astonishing design effort blew me away. 

At heart a race game, with deck building, worker placement and resource conversion elements. There are also significantly important timing and leeching elements reminiscent of Race for the Galaxy (in that taking advantage of what your opponents do can help you along a lot). All of these things come together in a way that not only feels fresh, it feels like a ton of game with not much rules density. That might be what I find most amazing - this is the kind of game that, in less skilled design and development hands, could easily have collapsed into a confused mess of mechanisms that work at cross purposes, instead of a game that feels like more than the sum of its parts. 

Highly recommended. Along with Ludonaute itself, which has now released two of my favourite games from the past year (this and Little Prince).

RATING: 8
心得:
組牌 競速的遊戲
感覺就是一開始想辦法組好一個前進的引擎
然後快速衝刺
卡牌隨機性有點高
規則:
*起始跟據玩家數拿印第安人 6-9-12-15-18，一個放版圖上，起始打開五張牌，照力量排序，小的在下面
*每個玩家起始一毛一食一裝備，一個印第安人
*玩家每回作一次強制行動跟兩個可選行動(順序隨便)
強制行動:出手上卡牌配人啟動數次，放印地安人到圖版上行動
可選 : 
1.扎營算天數收牌，一張手牌一天，如果探索員退到營後，不要移動營地
2.買一張人物牌，棄牌換裝備雇人一次最多只能棄一張
*使用口譯員的角色卡行動時，把遊戲圖板上的印地安人都聚集到這裡，這時玩家可以把印地安人放到自己的船上(數量自己決定)。
*可放置多個印地安人區，一次最多三個(馬跟船)。
*不能棄掉船上的資源來放新獲得的資源
*卡片能不能用都可以出，資源也可以選擇少拿。
*偵查員前進同位置時，往前放一格，扎營後退同一格時，後退一格。
*遊戲在有人扎營時超過終點就獲勝

RATING: 6
A 2 hour exercise in tedium. Sure, it has lots of solid Euro mechanic staples with a few twists so it at least provides something a bit novel. Sure, it works just fine. But really you're playing the same game about 7 or so times. Collect resources as efficiently as your neighbours allow (you can clone the resource icons they've used recently), convert into stuff that allow you to move. Move as far as you can, and when you're close to running out your deck set your camp to where you've moved to. End mini-game 1. Start again. Gather. Move. Camp. End mini-game 2. And so on. You can save some resources for next turn, but the more you save, the less your camp gets to move forward, so it's inefficient to prepare too much for the following mini-game. You can buy some souped up cards to allow you to gather and move more efficiently, but not too many cards as that prolongs each of your mini-games (as cards in hand also restrict moving your camp forward). So get a bit of soup and process, re-set, re-process, repeat, lather, rinse, repeat, ad-nauseum for 2 hours until someone thankfully wins. Now it's not all that bad, I've given it a 6 after all, but I guess people are liking it because there are different ways to set up your mini-game efficiency engine and try them out based on the cards that are available to buy and add to your hand (there's a whole bunch of luck there as well in what comes up when it's your turn and you can afford to buy). There's nice decisions as to how you want to power up your actions (you can use cards and/or workers to ramp up each action). And there's tactical stuff on how to take advantage of what other people have just done. But they're all minor variations on a theme. And speaking of theme, where's the EXPLORATION STUFF!!! It's a race game ... build an engine, process that engine, with tactical deviation to take advantage of what's left you, but process that engine. I'm really not sure I want to do that X times for 2 hours too often.

RATING: 9
Lewis & Clark is a race game, but it doesn't feel like one.  You play a card from your hand to determine your action for the the turn, whether that be moving, collecting resources, gathering indians, etc.  However, to get your cards back you have to camp, at which point you'll have to move backwards if you have too many resources, indians, or cards in your hand.  During the course of the game, you can acquire new and better cards, but you have to be careful that that doesn't make your hand even harder to manage.  This is a game that rewards careful multi-turn planning, so you end up with exactly as many resources you need, no more and no less.

(5+ plays)

RATING: 5

Edit: So I re-read the rules after not having played this game for a VERY long time. I missed one (somewhat) important rule for the game. You can trash a card from your hand and apply the equipment cost on the back of the card towards the purchase of new card. Maybe I was playing it correctly, but I don't recall that rule. Now it may not affect my rating entirely or even have that much of a game play change, but it is rather important and I'd like to play this again after trying it correctly. I'll re-rate the game again after trying. Currently it's a 5 BEFORE playing the correct way.

Update: So I played it correctly and solo. Playing it with the corrected rule did improve the game, but not significantly. Solo was fine, better than I expected, but it's literally just a timer for the game and it feels more like an optimization puzzle. I don't think my rating will change. Still looking for this game to go to a better home. I've given it plenty of chances to emerge now.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
A resource-conversion puzzle/game with a few novel (to me) features: 

*To gain resources or convert to better ones generally involves playing a pair of character cards, one to signify the resource type and the other flipped (and tucked under the first) with a multiplier icon (Red Indian) allowing the collecting/converting to be done 1, 2 or 3 times.  You can max up to 3 times by placing 1 or 2 Red Indian meeples from your expedition board. If you don't have a second character card you can use your meeples. (You gain more -unique- character cards by drafting at varying costs) 

With resource collection you count each time how many tabled cards match your desired resource, including your opponent(s) tableau (a la 7 Wonders) then multiply. 

So timed right you can scooped a lot, however this bounty needs to placed on your expedition board with limited space (you can buy-with resources-to expand your board), but the main consideration is the goods can slow the progress expedition party, which brings me to the focus of the game part: Be the first player to get your expedition party from the East side to the West side of the US.

*A lot of resource-conversion puzzle/games I've played is all about victory points, a fractured, scattered means of conveying status. Refreshingly in L & C status is simply done by moving a scout meeple up river and over mountains, again by means resource conversion via paired cards. However, like in 80 Days the status may be an illusion because when you decide to pick up your tabled cards, your scout has to go back a number of spaces determined by the weight the expedition carries and how many character cards in your hand at the time of your decision.

*Enhancing deck and resource management there is worker placement involving the multitasking meeples in the middle of the board, with some spots only allowing one meeple, reminding me of Stone Age

So in all a mix of familiar mechanics with some novel ones, with the visual race making me prefer this over types such as Agricola. The fact L & C can be played solo has kicked Agricola out to pasture. I wonder if 1 or 2 players may be the sweet spot as there is no  resource market competition or trading to maintain non-player interest, and although the different character cards provide some variation overall it is a humourless venture maybe best done alone.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 6
This game has that quick 'build your engine' thing that I normally like, but since it's a racing game, there are a lot of cool things to do, but all you SHOULD do is set up one or two good combos and then flog them repeatedly. The card playing mechanic is similar to Concordia, but in any given round you use half your cards for their actions and half to boost the strength of the first half. So what is it that makes this less interesting? It might be the lack of victory points. Getting your camp to the end is a win and that's that. It's an intellectual distinction, since I've played and enjoyed score track games that end when a player reaches X points, but I feel it here. There are also so many cool things, it seems a shame to force players to be tight with their decks.

:star: Art and components
:nostar: Innovative mechanics
:star: Handles 5 or more players
:star: Good with min or max players
:star: Low time between turns
:star: Comfortable complexity
:star: Re-playability
:nostar::nostar::nostar: Enjoyment
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 6
This is a great looking game with some serious deck and resource management. This makes playing the game very calculating because in this race game you really want to count every step. Progress in the mountains requires different resources than going forward at a river. And this carefull planning can be big fun.
A player's turn can be very fast or it can be very slow. The high number of cards, all with unique characters add atmosphere to the game. Unfortunately a bit too many of these cards come with unclear iconography.
Having played the game now twice, once with four and with five. I think the game plays greater with fewer numbers as there can be too much downtime as in between your turns there is hardly anything going on what may influence you. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Love this game. Very intense resource management where you need to utilise a number of different resources to build canoes and horses to race (first one there wins) from one side of America to the other. All without incurring progress penalties (you get moved back for holding too many resources and not playing all of your characters)! Because building and moving the canoes/horses has a lot of steps involved, there is a lot of planning needed as you decide which resources to get this turn, which character card or villager to use to get them and which new character card to get each turn.

You need to compete for the different characters with resources. These characters come out in random order each game from a deck, so this varies the strategy you will be able to use each game. Another nice twist.

Juggling all that makes for a very challenging and interesting game. And the artwork is very nice. Would look very good on the shelf!

RATING: 9
Took me some time to write this comment, but at least I can honestly say "Lewis & Clark" is a great game. Still, it should be mentioned that I've never tried it with more than two - we love this one with my wife & that's how we play it. 

But to the point. "Lewis & Clark" is a 100% eurogame. The theme is fine and the art amazing, but I never feel immersed in the world. In other words, it's just an exercise in optimization - something I personally love! There's some deck building here, some resource conversion - standard fare, as eurogames go. But it all creates a great feel of a real race. There are no points here, just be the first on the finish line and you've won. I like it - it feels fresh and makes the overall situation on the board transparent. You always know who's winning and who's probably out of contention, which can be both good and bad. I've heard complaints of huge downtime, but in 2p game it's not a problem. 

Overall, "Lewis & Clark" is a great game for all euro lovers. It's thinky and very replayable. Creating overpowered combos brings smile to your face, and while interaction is on a lower level (comes down to snatching a great card faster or denying your opponent resources), I wouldn't call it solitaire. Wife bought it for me as a birthday present, cause she loved the art, and the game never disappointed - turns out sometimes you CAN judge a game by its cover!

RATING: 9
This game drips with history, which I love. Components really well designed and the artwork is incredible--lovely to look at. Rulebook is well done. However the 30 minute/player estimate is laughable. Our first game, in which we learned the rules, took 5 hours for two players. There are so many possible choices, which is complicated by the fact that you can only take one action per turn and turn planning can be upset by the other players' actions--your careful plan for the next three turns can be destroyed by the time your turn comes around, meaning you'll have to start over planning. Downtime and AP will be a problem with more players. It's possible that opening moves and strategic lines will become apparent with more play, which would shorten game play. Until then, I would not try this with more than three players unless they already knew how to play.

RATING: 7.5
Lewis & Clark is very tactical Euro game with card drafting and hand management as the major mechanism. L&C features the currently very popular twist of different uses of a single card: you can either play the card for its effect or use it to "activate" other cards. Typical for a Euro game there is a lot of ressource conversion and also some worker placement. Hording a lot of ressources does not work here, since this will make you lose some progress on the journey track. I enjoy very much that you have to reduce anything to the absolutely necessary for your expedition. Although this is an almost abstract mechanism, it conveys the thematic feeling of an expedition very well.

I like about L&C that it is a race game with a clear objective. Unlike gaining VP for arbitrary actions, you have to find a way to effectively move up the river and through the mountains. Judging the available cards how you could utilize them to reach this goal is very entertaining. The game has a unique art-style and a beautiful map; I like if map and action spaces (Indian village) blend well into each other. 

There is very little player interaction and the label "Mulitplayer Solitaire" fits very well to L&C. But in this category it is among the best (depending on if you put Rosenberg's games in this category as well) and offers a very unique mix of different mechanisms. As there is only little interaction and each additional player adds some downtime, I think this is a very good 3 player game, still OK with 4, but should be avoided with 5.

I have the suspicion that the hanging back strategy (hoarding ressources and getting two boats in the beginning, but only move back 5 spaces from St. Louis) is very powerful and could be exploited (play cards and immediately rest), such that others will not benefit from your tableau. Further plays will show, if this is true. But even if this is a case, there are quite a few fixes for that (see this  [article=15256280]thread[/article]). [i]Update: With a gentlemen's agreement that you do not set camps in consecutive rounds while falling behind St. Louis (i.e. 3rd solution proposed in the thread), I do not think this to be a problem any more. Update2: Now that there is an official fix ([url=http://boardgamegeek.com/thread/1138777/can-hang-back-and-tech-strategy-be-beaten/page/4]link[/url]) for the hang back strategy, this should be no problem anymore.[/i]

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 9
Just a great experience with this historical euro game that throws in a splash of unique deck building.  I love the pull between strategy and tactics.  Your strategy seems upfront but the tactics comes in when you opponent goes and then there seems to be bargain.  there is almost an outwit your opponent aspect in the case.   Furthermore, the integration of the theme within the euro mechanic is great each aspect whether recruiting people or Indians or uses of resources they all really make sense and you get the feeling of building a caravan as you go.  All the strategy and tactics mix can create a situation conducive to AP and downtime so I wouldn't recommend this with more than 3 players unless you don't mind the downtime.  I would say this probably plays best with 2.  Anyway, just a fun venture and I look forward to trying it solo and playing more in general. 

Update:  there is really quite some variance to win this.  Two games, two completely different strategies both resulting in wins. It's really about finding a strategic engine based on the recruits that come up and then tactically tweaking that plan as more recruits come up but then you might decided to get rid of recruits and time can be crucial. There can be a real deck molding aspect to this game that I did not explore on my first play.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
One of the most interesting of the new games we got for Christmas 2013 (and we were very lucky to pick up a copy from a friend who happens to run a local store - everywhere else in the UK seemed to sell out within half an hour of getting their copies). A race game, powered by a combination of worker placement and deckbuilding - it manages to give a real feel of managing an expedition, in terms of judging when your people have outlived their usefulness and are now just slowing you down, and whether you should overload yourself with resources that you may not be able to use for a while.

The most interesting mechanic is that, although I mentioned deckbuilding, there is no luck of the card draw - all new cards gained go directly to your hand, where they remain until played. Also that each card needs another card (or some of your precious "workers") to be played with it in order for it to have any effect, so you constantly have to judge how to best use your cards. All in all, this is one of the most interesting and original games of recent years - while I haven't fallen in love with it on my 3 plays so far, I have found it intriguing and will certainly enjoy my future plays. But it's a real brain-burner, and even though I personally like brain-burners, this is not one I think I'd want to play every week. (And actually, we've not played for ages, and of course we've acquired several waves of shiny new games since then - L&C is I think too good, or at least too novel with much still to explore, to deserve to be totally forgotten about.)

RATING: 6
Não me diverti com o jogo, mas pensando a respeito dele, acho que merece mais partidas. O problema pra mim é que o jogo alia uma mecânica muito diferente de uso das cartas e dos meeples, com a qual você tem que se acostumar nas primeiras rodadas, com as cartas de deckbuilding, que são todas diferentes e fazem entrar informação nova no jogo toda rodada. Sendo que essa informação é colocada numa iconografia de difícil leitura.

Isso atrapalhou bastante a fluidez do jogo e consequentemente a diversão. Se jogar ele de novo em breve, a mecânica não vai ser mais um problema, a iconografia vai ficar mais fácil e as cartas vão ser mais familiares. Vou conseguir raciocinar o jogo sem precisar lutar para entender o jogo. E aí, acho que vamos gostar bem mais.

O que não significa que ele não tenha problemas: o principal pra mim é o pula-carniça que faz uma jogada de um movimento virar uma de cinco. Se isso for tão presente como o trem da alegria da partida que joguei, meio que transforma um jogo bem cerebral num troço meio zoeira.

RATING: 5.2
This is a race game where you race over land and water. You have a scout, who does the racing and a base camp that keeps track of your last location. Although this is not a deck builder per se, you can add cards to your deck to improve your selections. You keep playing cards from your hand until you decide to camp. However, when you camp, you can end up going backward if you have too much stuff or cards in your hand.
While parts of this game are interesting, what put me off is that you need to do a myriad of things and this results in moving a few spaces.
I felt like I was asking a girl to dance and she told me first I had to do fifty pushups, run three laps around the room, stand on my head, then do the Curly shuffle before she'd dance with me.
Not in my future acquisition plans. 

Snipe hunt.

I won, because halfway through the game I stopped proceeding and just sat there buying cards while my opponent kept advancing, thinking she was winning. Once I had my engine, I raced the rest of the way to the end.

Liked: Simple winning mechanism vs other worker placement games, the option to choose between worker placement and engine building mechanics, being able to use shared resources.

Less superficially, the game does feel a bit too much like several games put together. While the system interact, you can almost choose to play primarily with the worker placement mechanism, or primarily as an engine builder. Balance between those feels like it might be off.

RATING: 10
Easy to learn, but quite the brain burner. A mostly tactical game that requires players to respond to cards as they become available for in-game purchase. The hand management mechanic is brilliant and rewards efficiency (it is a race game after all). 

The map and card art looks great, but cards are thin and rather poor quality compared to modern board gaming standards. Definitely sleeve the cards, even though they won't get shuffled much. 

I noticed some complaints about the length of this game. For us this has almost become a meaty filler due to how fast it goes. As a 2 player game we can reliably be done in under 45 minutes.  

Update: This is one of the best (if not the best) board games that I own as far as game play is concerned, but probably the worst game I own as far as the quality of the board is concerned. I have tried hard to cajole my warped board into some semblance of a flat shape, but without much success.

RATING: 7
Played once with 4P during the BGC Retreat 2014. Lewis & Clark is a pseudo-racing game which involves a clever mechanism of hand management. It is clever because you have to pair up cards in your hand to an optimum effect, while considering the cards your opponents have already played down. The cards you play are mostly used to gather resources, which are then converted into movement on the expedition track.

While the game has some clever ideas, I can't help but feel that it is a bit too convoluted for its own good. It's also a little prone to downtime as your plans can be scuttled when your opponent decides to set up camp and eliminates resources you thought you would have during your turn. Also, the game runs a little long due to this downtime. I would love to get another game in some time to justify this rating.

RATING: 9
Played once at GenCon 2013 with a friend; rules overview was provided by Anne-Cécile from Ludonaute.  This is a worker-placement game with some deck-building, but doesn't not involve and victory points!  What?! Instead, this is a racing game where players compete to be the first to traverse the river and mountains to be the first to reach the West Coast.  While moving along the river and over the mountains is what has to be done to win the game, there are many different combinations of resources that allow the necessary movement depending on the members you have recruited into you expedition. For example, the starting cards make it obvious that canoes are necessary to move on water and you get those by using wood. However, later cards will allow you to "trade" with the Indians, so all of a sudden horses or meat can be traded for canoes. Similar things can happen with crossing mountains. The cards are all shuffled and come out in a random order each time, so there will be a different mix each time.  

This is an innovative game design.  My play was 2-player and I really enjoyed it. It was very tense and it's always good to be able to watch what the other player is doing and try to adjust or mess with him. Based on the length of the play though, I'd probably keep this at 2 or 3 players.

SOS Titanic and Lewis & Clark were the two best games that I played at GenCon 2013. They will be instant buys for when they are released (sadly, there weren't any copies for sale at GenCon).
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.1
This game you can tell that it's well designed. Everything mechanically makes sense and works together in harmony. It's a brain burner for sure so only play with the right people. I think 1 - 3 is the only player count it's designed for. It has a bad reputation for being too long and I think it's because people have tried playing it at 4 - 5 which shouldn't have been written on the box.

RATING: 7
2 plays, 4er

This is a pretty cool hand management deck-building (sort of) game where you race down the river, cross mountains here and there, and try to cross the finish line fastest. You have to remember it's a race, and not get caught up in acquiring all the cool cards (every card in the deck is unique!). But you do have to acquire something to help you along, then make most efficient use of it. Sort of like Ascension, with the constantly changing display, but you only acquire a few cards and then spend the rest of the time actually using the deck. I think?

I would like to play this again, even though it took quite some time. I think it should be faster with repeated play, and probably better with just 2 or 3 players.

[b]--- Summary ---[/b]
Players each have a camp marker and scout token starting at one end of the river. 

Everyone starts with the same deck of cards, each of which allow a different type of action: collect one of 4 basic resources, call a Pow Wow (collect Indian tokens from the board), convert basic resources to advanced goods, move your Scout down the river by paying some combo or resources, etc. The backs of these show 1-3 Indians: when playing a card, play another face down as well (and/or add Indian tokens) -- the number of Indians is the number of times you can take that action. On your turn, you take one action -- either playing card(s) to your display, or spending an Indian meeple to take an action on the board. Many actions have you looking at icons in your display and/or that of your neighbors' displays.

You can also buy one new card from the card row, adding it to your hand.

Eventually, you'll make camp: move your Scout backwards down the river a number of spaces depending on how many cards remain in hand, and goods/Indians remain in storage. Advance your camp marker to your Scout's new position. Then return all cards (and Indian tokens) from your display to your hand (and personal storage board). The first player to set camp past the finish line wins the game immediately!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8

RATING: 7
1-5, 120m
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
This is a racing game and probably my favorite racing game I've played.  I'm not usually a fan of racing games but this one has a lot of other game mechanics mixed in.  Along with the racing mechanics that dictate the actual goal of the game the gameplay is made up of hand management, role selection, and worker placement.

In this game all players are explorers trying to make it up the river and set up camp at or past the finish line first.  They do this by playing different cards from their hands to collect different resources and take actions that move them up river.  There are also "Indian" meeples that are placed on board spaces in worker placement fashion.  After cards are played from your hand they remain out of your hand until you set up camp to take back all your cards.  This also sets up camp at the position you have moved down the river but first you have to lose time based on the boats you have filled and cards remaining in your hand.  This makes for some interesting decisions since you need to balance the efficiency of your actions with how much time you lose when setting up camp to take all your cards back.

The one problem I can see though is with one of the main reasons I don't care for a lot of racing games.  The first person to cross the finish line wins immediately and there is no bonus given to players who go after the starting player.  This means the player(s) who go earlier in turn order have a distinct advantage.  There are certain cards that alleviate that but those cards won't necessarily come out or be available to the later acting players.  The game should also be played with larger player numbers since many of the cards in the game are artificially handicapped when playing with just 2.

I still greatly enjoyed this game.  I like worker placement and varied powers but the potential imbalance in a game that ends this way worries me.  I may lose a lot of this enthusiasm for the game if I notice this in a future play.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: N/A
Like in every race game there's something that turns me off. It's not a problem of the game it's my own feeling about these kinds of games. Mainly because once you get to learn how to explore the map and deal with its difficulties, like surpassing the mountains and which cards are of better use to do so, you get the whole picture and then you have the pure race game with no more surprises, like you do when you're playing for the first time. It's like playing Pandemic. Once you figure out which powers you should use and when, then you have a boring game.
In Lewis and Clark, although there is much more complexity than in an average race game, players seem to learn sooner or later the best way to play it and suddenly, all the charm and fun vanishes.
But it is a cool game and it certainly has its merits. I'm sure there are lots of people cheering for this game but unfortunately I'm not one of them. Once again, it's more like my personal distaste than a game problem.

(N)

RATING: 6.5
After I read the rules I have to admit I didn't want to play. The game seemed unintuitive, messy, and unnecessarily complicated. While I still think it's not the most intuitive game, this really is a great game. 

It's hard for me to articulate exactly what makes this game good. I really like that the game isn't a point salad game where you don't really ever know how well you are doing in relation to the other players. I like that there are so many different options and paths to victory. No turn ever feels wasted because there is so much that you need to accomplish and no right way to pursue it.

Feel slightly uncomfortable that Indians are used as resources and that they are represented by red meeples. But all the people in the game, including Indians, have names and bios. So the game does a good job in being really careful about what could be a sensitive issue. 

The beautiful presentation tries to mask the fact that the mechanics are not very thematic or intuitive. It frequently brought me in and out of the game similar to how a bad movie script can take you in and out of the movie. 

All in all a tense game with a lot of decisions to make, but a bit thematically detached which makes the experience kind of jarring.

RATING: 7.5
[Asmodee 2014 second edition] {Exploration; worker placement, deck-building, racing; scalable} Lewis & Clark is a very good game - it is thematic, engaging and fun.  The game mechanics meld together seemlessly.  I love this game.

This game is particularly refreshing because we don't burn our brains with convoluted scoring rules.  This game is simple - just be the first one to set up a camp at the goal line.

The game components are good. I love the illustration of cards - pretty and thematic.  The game would even be more perfect if shaped tokens are used instead of the vanilla wood cubes - actually, the choice of color is confusing and I can't understand why pink is food, etc.

The replayability of this game is very good.  The randomness of character cards and the varaible set up of the route.

The rulebook is well illusrated and written.  It is easy to understand the core mechanics easily.  I wish player aid cards are provided and a reference sheet (in A4 size) for all character cards.  It is so clumsy to check the rulebook for the functions of character cards.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Race game which cleverly combines worker placement and deck building. Players compete against each other to be the first to reach the Pacific Ocean following the route taken by Meriwether Lewis and William Clark. This is achieved by travelling along rivers and through mountains using modes of transport such as canoes and horses purchase from local Native Americans. Workers are placed to acquire resources and characters can also be obtained to bring more powerful actions. The key to the game is to carefully balance the right number of resources and knowing when to move along the route. Too many unused resources slows the player down or can result in them going backwards. The winner is the first to complete the journey. 'L&C' combines two well worn mechanisms very well and is cleverly designed. It requires a serious amount of though at times to figure out the optimum time to attempt to move along the route and provides quite a unique gaming experience. There's nothing else like this out there at the moment.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
Sound Euro. Has a number of different resources which can be traded up for actions, actions being more efficient resource changing, more resource gathering (characters), more resource storage (boats) and most important, more efficient travelling (canoes, characters). Usually has more than one method of establishing this, leaving you with a diverse set of options to tackle the problems at hand. Main problem of course getting ahead as it is a race game, but there is a game mechanic forcing you to empty your hand and resources (as much as possible) by the end of your turn or you will be penalized.

The diverse set of characters appearing in every game makes that this game has very high replayability and the racing aspect, the fact that resource gathering directly keys in to what opponents are doing and the ever present scarity of devoted action spaces makes this a more interactive euro as normal, even though there is no direct trading or spatial effect (akin to Terra Mystica) involved. Good thing as well is that there is more theme as usual in a Euro - the mechanics and theme match well and don't feel tacked on.

If you like Euro's, a bit of interaction and don't want to spend a lot of euro's on getting/trying them all, this is a very accessible, surprisingly deep and highly replayable game that will keep you involved for a long time. Recommended!

RATING: 7.3
Man, has this game gotten some buzz and attention. Naturally I had to try it!
This game combines Stone Age style worker placement with deck building and a race element. All things I enjoy. And it's put together quite nicely, with some nice twists. There's enough meaningful choices to make. It's a fun game. I enjoyed playing it. Until the second half. Once everyone got his deck up and running in such a way that he was satisfied with it, the game became very repetitive. We just had to keep going through the motions until someone crossed the end point of the river. Maybe it had to do with group think, but I don't think so. At a certain point, it doesn't become beneficial to get too many new cards. Especially since the cards are one big mixed deck in stead of stacked decks that gradually give better cards at higher costs. That would keep the game interesting longer I think.
It's a fine game, but I don't think - judging from this first game - that this is a great game.
One more thing: please stop printing your games in China. I know the components in this game are decent (by Chinese norms) but I was put off by it. Especially the flimsy cards.

RATING: 9
Really enjoyed thoughtful and Tense game, having read the book "Undaunted Courage" by Stephen Ambrose I was worried that the game would be too abstract and the theme and feel of the story would be lost, however, was pleasantly surprised, the game does feel like the journey depicted on the book, sending the scouting party into the unknown, spending time with the natives trading and struggling to glean information about the way ahead. Yes it's an abstract view of this but all the characters are there and with their different abilities they do feel like characters.

If anyone wants to understand what Lewis & Clark were up against check out the paintings of Charles Fritz, he has done 100 paintings following their journey.

RATING: 5.5
Takes the engine building/VP race of most Euro games and cleverly (and surprisingly, seemingly originally) simply converts it into a physical race. The deck building is a nice addition here, whereas usually I dislike it. Perhaps the non-shuffling of your cards, as also seen in Concordia, is the key.

The track can be nicely varied using the terrain tokens provided which allows for repeat play. 

This is a charming little game that should only be played with non-AP players. If you take more than a minute on any single turn, I get to stab you in the eye with a fork.

Update: I can concerns about the longevity of this game, and 4 plays in, I think I've seen everything there is to see and will be moving on. I'll leave my rating as is, but if I end up playing it again in the near future my rating may well take a hit.

Update: After further plays this has become a case of build a lean deck and run it. See if it's more efficient than your opponents. It has become the same (dull) exercise that all other deck builders are. It's a well crafted deck builder, it's just not for me.

Rating down from 7.3 to 5.5.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
This really is something unique. You have to keep an eye on your neighbours production to possibly increase your own production, then you have the ingenious use of the  cards, which you can use to make an action or strengthen an action from a card just played by putting it "hidden" underneath, further being able to strengthen the action cards with indians,with indians & acard in addition or just with indians combined with a deckbuilding element that really stands out from the crowd of deckbuilders. The usual deckbuilder allows you to more or less mindlessly add cards to your deck and somehow this will transform into your tactical scheme for that particular session. You REALLY do not want to clogg your deck here at all and have to be much more considerate about which card(s) to add to your deck. Of course this also means to give much more thought in regards as to which cards you want to discard. Discarding in "Lewis & Clark" means to trash it. Another key factor is that you decide when to take your cards back on your hand (unless there is no other option) with every card you still have on your hand costing you time. Having too many cards can be hurting you, since it will take long time before you get your options back on the hand plus you're giving your opponents nice production boosters with the cards that are already in the play area and finally a big deck will slow down your expedition. This and actually all the mechanics in this game are so very very clever (which I was definitely not during our first session), I am totally enarmoured with this game and want to play it again. I can't think of a more innovative release from the Essen 2013 crop. 
_______________________________________________________

The second take at this was somewhat disillusioning. It showed that we played the game wrong the first time,but the first time we played the game felt much much smoother even though rules were interpreted faulty.The second time around the game was in parts painfully brainy not to mention that the correct rules were found by coincidence when reading an example of a game situation. Which doesn't speak in favor of the rule books' quality. I still like the game and appreciate that it stands out from the lot of the new releases, but due to the rules and the tedious experience this time I had to adjust my rating: 9->7.5

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Really good game. Having boats that will slow you down if they get too full is a really clever way to deal with players who would normally hoard resources. This means that you always have to carefully plan what to do so that you don't have extra resources leftover. There is also a real sense of progression as you gain cards that are more efficient and navigate along the scoring track. 

I've only got a few issues with the game. One is that I don't care for careful planning, as I usually like decisions that are more tactical. One or two mistakes can really set you back and you can usually feel that you're far behind even if you're still close to the other players on the score track.
The other gameplay issue, and this coincides with the first, is that it can be a little too thinky. There are two optional actions to take on a turn and you always have to think about the best order to take them. Also, the cards have multiple uses so you always have to consider which card to use for which purpose. This results in player who are normally not very AP-prone having to take really long turns and the game can drag on.

The last thing is that the production seems fairly cheap, and others on the forums have pointed this out as well. The company even admitted that they intentionally made a lower-quality product in order to cut costs. Now, the game is perfectly playable and there are no major issues, but the game just doesn't feel like most modern board game that we're all used to. The cards are bowed, which is not a huge problem but just annoying, and the board and box feel like they may not hold up as well as other games.

Sold.

RATING: 5.5
First Impression:

A unique hybrid of an economical efficiency/racing game.

Lots of choices, lots of strategies and lots of replayability are the mark of a good game. The game features mature deck building (not traditional but still far better than anything I've played, including A Few Acres of Snow) and tough resource management (the only game I've played where more is as bad as less). Tying your economical engine with a race ties the pace of the game to the abilities of its players and transforms the final moments of the game into moments of agony and excitement as opposed to "final round let's count vps". The theme ties very well, creates the feeling of an adventure and the little details all over the game cry out that the game is a labour of love.

Player interaction is way too subtle and though it does make some difference it's much less than I'd like. You can't do much if another player has built a better "engine" than you other than wait to lose. It's also prone to analysis paralysis, depending on how clear a strategy each player has. The theme can leave some players indifferent initially, but it's one of those few games that manage to support the theme, as opposed to be supported by it. The racing part doesn't create agony as there is no push your limits aspect to the game.

The game overall is good, to the point that it makes you wonder why noone thought to tie an econ engine with a race before. I liked it, I'm looking forward to play it more but I didn't love it as the winner is usually obvious from midgame.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7

Lewis & Clark is a race game that is card driven.  It's a resource gathering game where gathering more resources than you can use is punished.  And it's a worker placement game where the number of workers you get is determined by how many workers are on the board.  Workers are in short supply at the beginning of the game. And again, having more workers than you can use is punished.  And worker placement is actually a very minor part of the game, being dwarfed by the card play.  

The card aspect is interesting as cards are both abilities to be used or the strength of the ability.  So neat cards can be used as a really cool ability, or as a lot of power for some other card.  So often you'll get a cool card, but not be able to max it out.  

The race aspect of the game is that you move along the path and "set up camp".  Setting up camp is the only way to get your cards back into hand.  It's also the way that you call your camp token up to where your explorer token is... except if you have too many resources, indians or cards in hand, you get penalized and have to move your explorer backwards before your camp moves.

Making the indian pawns red colored, i.e. redskins, was a really poor design choice.

I really enjoyed this and look forward to trying it again.

RATING: 8
I was really impressed by this game. The mechanism of having to play two cards at once, one for the action and one for the multiplier is very clever. The scoring is well disguised as a race track, and the worker placement, Indian collection, and time mechanisms are beautifully integrated. The card mechanism of faux deck-building is one I have never seen before, as you have access to all of your cards, but retrieving them comes at a cost. Very well thought out. I had expected this to be another typical worker placement game with little new to offer, but I was wrong. Probably the most original resource collection and worker placement game I have played.

RATING: 7.7
I had high expectation for this game. I was hoping to play a great and complex racing game, and i got disappointed. This is not a racing game. A racing game need steady progress like flowing water, for immersion feeling, but here the progress is like refill and draining a bucket for few huge step forward.

In essence, this is an engine building game, using cards and worker placement as mechanic, with a twist. The racing aspect itself is simply inherent to the theme. With these in mind, it is actually a well-designed game. High production value as well, with excellent artwork and the box insert are especially great.

Too bad it doesnt really fit to my taste, so this game is leaving my collection.

RATING: 8
I do like the game; 'Lewis and Clark', about exploring the state of Louisiana after Napoleon sold it to the Americans and these two gents were sent to do the task. Players take the role of individual expeditions seeking to be first to locate their camp past the Pacific coast using the equipment and resources they can muster. Effectively, players trade resources to build canoes and use horses to travel across river and mountain and also to encourage Indian support and trade for additional cards. There are over fifty of these so don't expect to understand the ramifications of all. I think there were a few misinterpretations from the game only having previously being played with two where presumably not all of the cards were deployed. I think there is a flaw in the game, as I understand it from observation, and that is that when there are more than one or more players in the mountain pass, the first player to take all the Indians from the pool, using activation cards to do so, the other players will be severely hampered, Yes, penalties are incurred for unused cards, Indians and unused resources, but when your opponents are not ,moving at all, that's a small price to pay. I would play this game again and again as I like the theme. I do feel one of the cards I had was not  interpreted correctly and I did check the wording after and that did make the game slightly less enjoyable. And yet, like I say, I'd play a nicely themed game again, nonetheless and I suspect that's a good endorsement despite the one minor glitch.

RATING: 7
Is Lewis and Clark a soulless cube pusher with little interaction? Yes, so if that's a deal breaker avoid this game. Also, it's probably not worth playing with more than 3 as the downtime would just be too much (the 2 player game is great). Those cautions aside, Lewis and Clark is a wonderful game that combines deck building with race game and throws in some worker placement for good measure. Every game is different as you must base your strategy on the cards that are available for purchase (only a subset of the entire deck is seen in a given game). The fact they provided tokens you can use to alter the geography of the board is a nice touch that should enhance replayability even more. I've already played Lewis and Clark 5+ times, and don't see myself tiring anytime soon.

RATING: 6
This is a tough one for me to bash. The cards and components are beautiful, the deck building and tableau building mechanisms are wonderfully executed and even the manual is well done, making a somewhat complex game easy to understand and the flavor text and historical background in the last few pages. I adore it.

But the game itself...Well, it's fun and challenging at first. But after about three games, you start to realize that with only two terrain types, it starts to become more about building a small tight engine than actually exploring any interesting card interactions. Worse, once someone gets an engine put together, they usually run away with the game. There is no way to slow them down, so usually you know who is going to win 2 or 3 turns before it actually happens. That's a real drag and ultimately what made me decide to trade this guy away.

It's a bummer, because it was one of my favorite productions of 2013.

RATING: 9.8

RATING: 9.5
An excellent hybrid of many genres, players much do much with little on each turn. Each round, one must decide which cards to activate and which to turn face-down, as all around the table choose between activating their most powerful cards a limited number of times, or activating their weaker cards many times. The game can slow as players reach the mountains and must reconsider their method of travel, though experienced players will plan for the mountains well before their scout advances that far, ensuring that their path to the Pacific is uninterrupted. Regardless, the decision of when to pare down one's expedition and continue with a skeleton crew is a treat for those who love engine-builders where efficiency is more valuable than sheer power, and can be the deciding factor in close games.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Racing game with a unique interaction that allows actions to become more powerful based other players using similar actions. The time element forces players to consider exactly what they want to do forces players to play lean, collecting the minimum amount of resources necessary to achieve their actions or else have to move backward along the route.

The action cards a different every game so there are always different action available to advance the expedition, but despite the different actions, players are essentially doing the same thing. Everyone needs the same resources to advance on rivers and the same resources to advance over mountains, and the cards that allow for both advancements are mostly similar.

There is minor player interaction that mostly involves collecting resources or taking additional action points. Although minor, it's enough to matter that you have to consider what your opponents will do and how that will affect your plan in the next several turns.

Biggest downfall is the huge amount of forward planning involved in the game, leading to some intense analysis paralysis. As that plan unfolds, we constantly felt like we made a huge mistake that drastically set us back. In fact, one big mistake can potentially set a player back 4 turns behind everyone else (which happened in our game).

You can improve your team by building more boats to transport goods or natives, by hiring new teammembers, and by letting other teammembers go. Your scout runs out ahead of you, but everytime you set up camp, he might have to double back if you haven't moved efficiently enough. 
Great art and good thematic integration: this is a great game.

RATING: 8
Though the core "hand building" component of L&C is pretty much the same as Concordias the focus here on building combos in your hand is much more interesting... though also much more brain burny.  Balancing how you use your hand against what items and people you end the turn with to keep your camp moving forward just adds to the puzzle and makes this a lot of fun who enjoy planning a few moves ahead, maximizing your moves and occasionally having your plans foiled unintentionally (and sometimes intentionally) by your opponents.  I just love the art and theme but its a bit too much to explain and grok for what I'm going for in most of my collection these days.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.2
Today there suddenly came to opportunity to play Lewis & Clark, a game we noticed just before Spiel 13, and we hoped we would be able to play it in Essen. Unfortunately all the tables were occupied with players all of the time. Today we went to the Belgium version of Spiel 13, called Spel 13. There was one table with Lewis & Clark on it, and as we passed and had a look, we were asked to play the game. We took the offer, and after the game was explained to us, we started playing. In the first couple of rounds, I was disappointed in the game. Mainly because of the way the game was explained to us. The guy who was explaining, did teach us the game good, that is, he told us everything there is to know about this game. The only minor point, is the fact that he gave is the idea, that he had to catch a train in 10 minutes, but that his boss told us, to explain the game to us, before he could leave. But slowly the game-mechanism became clear, and it evolved in a very nice, smooth and almost easy game. Each round, a player can do one action. He has only a choice between two different ones. Play a card or play an Indian. That’s it. When he plays a card, the card has to be activated. This can be done by placing the back of another card besides the card or by placing an Indian (1, 2 or 3) on the card. The back of the card shows 1 to 3 Indians on it. The amount of Indians, are telling you, how many times the card can be activated. And there is exactly where the trick is in this game. A very simple card, can become very power-full this way. But you don’t want it to be too power-full, because it can kick you back on the river, because your boats became too heavy. By placing the back of a card, you’ll ‘loose’ another possible action, so you’ll have to look carefully which back of card you’re playing. Some cards for gathering resources will look at your neighbors cards to determine how many resources you’ll gather. By doing it like this, there becomes interaction into the game. The interaction, is also one of the biggest minor points of this game. Because the action your neighbor took, might influence your move, you want to wait before you start thinking what to do. And as this is a game of calculation and strategy, it slows the game down. Also the hexagon tokens which represent buffalo’s, wood, stone and skins are a bit dull, and tear the theme down. I’ve seen some stickers already that can be placed onto the hexagon tokens, and they improve the game-experience. Besides the two minor drawbacks, this is a game that I could recommend to everyone.
